Charles wrote
> > > > Austin is *a* city
> > > > Austin is *the* capital
> > > > Austin is beautiful
> 
> > Can you explain what the significant differences are?
> 
> The indefinite article means "one of possibly many,
> and one which has not been discussed recently".
> You can figure out the definite and the adjectival uses.
> 

I think we can discount the "one of possibly many" 
meaning of 'a', as "'the' city" is also "one of possibly many." 

So, it's 'the', if it's already been mentioned, and 'a' if it's
not previously known or specified. So, that means the 
context decides which is appropriate. Therefore, it is
redundant.

Saluta,
Nick

From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Thu,  1 Nov 2001 15:52:19 -0800

> I think that we must use "plu" or "u" for the starting of noun-phrase.
> The three phrases in Russian are very similiar. They don't use any word for
> meaning of article. And in glosa they should use only "u" for singular and
> nothing else.

I agree. U/PLU are just singular/plural markers, not really articles.
Asians will prefer to elide them in many cases. To express definiteness
requires different particles (determiners). Any determiner can serve
to mark the beginning of a noun phrase (mainly, the direct object).

Subject: Re: [glosalist] not all, but some letters.
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>

At 15:52 01/11/2001 -0800, Charles wrote:

>> I think that we must use "plu" or "u" for the starting of noun-phrase.

>> The three phrases in Russian are very similiar. They don't use any word for

>> meaning of article. And in glosa they should use only "u" for singular and

>> nothing else.

>

>I agree. U/PLU are just singular/plural markers, not really articles.

>Asians will prefer to elide them in many cases. To express definiteness

>requires different particles (determiners). Any determiner can serve

>to mark the beginning of a noun phrase (mainly, the direct object).

>

*  Plu Amika,

     I agree that the meaning of "determinants" and the functions of "u" and "plu" do not necessarily match up.

     However, because for me, an English-speaker, the "a" and "the" of English are part of my thinking pattern, I have uncritically accepted the Glosa dictionary listing:-

        U  =  the, a

      PLU  =  the (plural)


     And, in general, I find no difficulty in locating these particles at the start of NOUN PHRASES where they seem appropriate.  But, where a numerator is used, it is fairly obvious that either U or PLU are not needed.


    Plu ju-an pa trans u via.    ( a number of boys; one road )


    Tri ju-an trans bi via.      ( 3 boys; 2 roads ) 


BUT  Plu tri ju-an; qi habita u proxi viki, 

    [the  3  boys  who live-in the nearby village]


    pa trans plu bi via; alelo na viki. 

   [did cross the 2 roads (which are) beside our village]


   Did I use ~plu~ as a "determinant" in both of these cases, or was I simply trans-scribing my English usage into Glosa?

   If there are only three boys in the next village, these are they.

   If there is a particular pair of roads running beside our village, then these are the same two roads.


   But hey: the next village is full of boys, and our village is at a cross-roads of at least five roads.  So, I might say:-


     Tri ju-an; qi habita u proxi viki, 

    [ 3  boys  who live-in the nearby village]


     pa trans bi via; alelo na viki. 

   [did cross 2 roads (which are) beside our village]


       We can't actually specify the boys, but we do know they are three from the nearby village; and, they did actually cross two of the roads, which run by our village.


   Perhaps in English there is also such mixed usage of "the" and "a": determinant and comfy 'noun-marker.'

 - - - - - - - - - - - - - - - - - - - - - 

   Real problems used to present themselves for me in Glosa, when I specifically wanted to use the "a" [one of] function, and also when, in English, I would naturally say "some" [an amount of].

   Nowadays, I use ~uno~ [= one of the] to specify an un-named singularity.

  EG  Uno andra pa kine-auto to un antero de u grega.

     [a man did move himself to(position) the front of the group]

      A man stepped to the front of the group.


   We do not know who he was, so we cannot say "the" man, but he was probably the group's spokesman.


   And for years I thought there could be a Glosa word, like ~des~ [of the] to translate the general usage of "some" in English.  But, in trying to fit in with the Ashford dictionaries, I decided that Glosa did, in fact have two "somes"

   IE   plura = several   ......  for COUNTABLES, and

        mero de = part of   ....  for NON-COUNTABLES.


 EG  Tem intra u kuko-ka, plura de plu gyna pa fago (u) mero de u kasea.

    [while in the kitchen several of the women ate a part of the cheese] 

     While in the kitchen, some women ate some of the cheese.


     Well, this sort of word game keeps me happy ... and it might be good Glosa as well.


Saluta, 


Robin Gaskell

At 15:30 01/11/2001 -0800, Charles wrote:

>> ???? wrote:

>> So, that means the

>> context decides which is appropriate. Therefore, it is

>> redundant.

>

>Read up on "determiners" and "syntax".

>

*   All true.

    But isn't it a little terse, Charles?

    I have not followed the thread, so don't know who made the prime statement.  I do not know which "it" we are talking about.


    However, the general rule in Glosa is that context is highly significant, because, with the lack of morphological grammar, the placement of the words is more important in Glosa than in most other languages.


    The gist on "determiners" is that 'U' and 'PLU' are quite handy as NP markers, and probably should be used if there is nothing else to 'flag' the NOUN PHRASE. 

    However, if you have other words (more substantial ones) leading up to the 'noun' (= substantive word functioning as a noun), then 'U' and 'PLU' can happily be elided - as long as the remaining words leave the reader/listener in no doubt about plurality, or otherwise ... if it was the intent of the utterer to be specific about this.


    EG  Kloro dendro tegu kumu, iso tele de uno-pe pote vide.

        Green tree(s) cover hill(s) as far as one   can  see.


   On the simple functioning of syntax, and on the sequencing of words within NOUN and VERB PHRASES, please check out Seminars #1 & #2, if you have not already done so.


Saluta,


Robin Gaskell

_A first Seminar on Glosa____________________________R. Gaskell Sept,1995_
               (With apologies, in English)
WORDS
        Words in Glosa represent concepts, and are really `concept/words.'
They represent ideas, but, depending on their position in the sentence,
and their relationships to other words, they may represent that idea in its
solid form (noun), active form (verb), or as a modifier of another idea
(adjective, adverb).

        The words do not change for reasons of grammar (system for getting
understanding from words): this means that words can be used, within
reason, as any part of speech, but have no letters added to indicate this
useage (no Part-of-Speech markers); also, the words which act as verbs,
`verbs', have no endings added to them (no inflections) to give added
understanding of how, or when, the action is taken.

        The order of the words (syntax) gives us the meaning in Glosa; the
language can be described as having `syntax-based grammar.'

PHRASES
        The phrase (small group of words) is the basic unit of sentence
construction in Glosa.  A small group of words might include a thing and
some description of it (noun phrase), or the group might include an action
and some modifications to this action (verb phrase).  A small group of
words at the start of a Glosa sentence might qualify the action of the
sentence (adverbial phrase).

        Within each phrase, each word is modified by the one before it, and
there is a gradual increase in importance of the words, with the main
concept/word (`noun' or `verb') as the last word of the phrase.
                e.g.  the three fast, loud red cars                  NP
                      plu tri celero fo-sono rubi vagona

                e.g.  were quickly end excitedly talking loudly      VP
                      pa du celero e excita fo-sono dice

                e.g.  while running     (relates to  --->  verb)     AdvP
                      tem kursi

        In speech, phrases are said as one group, often with a raising then
lowering of intonation (pitch), from start to finish; in written form, if
it seems necessary for the understanding of meaning, the start and finish
of phrases, is indicated using punctuation (commas).

CLAUSES
        A clause is a larger group of words containing two (NP + VP) or
more phrases, and such a simple two or three phrase clause can make up the
whole sentence (major clause).  However, a clause - which always includes
at least one verb phrase - might add meaning to the action of the
sentence (Adverbial Clause), might give more description to one of the
things in a sentence (Adjectival Clause), or it could be a group of words,
including a `verb,' used in the place that you would normally find a noun
phrase (Noun Clause).

      e.g.  The boy arrived.              U ju-an pa ariva.            MC

      e.g.  The girl went home.           U ju-fe pa ki a fe domi.     MC

      e.g.  The man will eat the meal.    Un andra fu vora u vora.     MC

      e.g.  She spoke so loudly that she was heard.
            Fe pa ta sono dice ke fe pa gene ge-audi.                AdvC
                          ~~~~ <-- ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~

      e.g.  The girl went home after she had eaten a good meal.
            U ju-fe pa ki a fe domi, po fe pra vora u boni vora.     AdvC
                       ~~   <---     ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
      e.g.  The boy, who was fat and badly dressed, arrived.
            U ju-an; qi pa es paki e mali ge-vesti, pa ariva.        AdjC
              ~~~~~ <-- ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
      e.g.  I like to get away from the house.
                  (getting)
            Mi amo gene ab u domi.                                     NC
                   ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
      e.g.  The farmer hoped that it would rain.
            Un agri-pe pa spera: ke id sio pluvi.                      NC
                       ~~~(V)~~  ~~~~(O)~~~~~~~~

SEQUENCE IN A CLAUSE
        The normal sequence in Glosa is the same as in English:-
 Main Clause =   noun phrase    -    verb phrase     -    noun phrase
                  (SUBJECT)            (VERB)              (OBJECT)
            Doer of the action    Action being done    Thing acted upon
           e.g.     He                 posts               a letter.
                    An                 posta               u grama.

        There are three main variations to this: clauses with no object
(intransitive verb); clauses where, for reasons of style, the speaker,
or writer, wishes to emphasise the object by placing it first (Passive
Voice); and Noun Clauses which have no subject (infinitive verb).
           e.g.    She (NP)    sang. (VP)                 Fe pa kanta.
                 (SUBJECT)    (VERB)

           e.g.   The letter       was posted        by the man.
                   U grama      pa gene ge-posta     ex un andra.
                               (did get got-post)
                   (OBJECT)         (VERB)                (SUBJECT)

           e.g.                  - running (=to run)     on wet grass
                                    kursi              epi ge-hidra gra
                                    (VERB)                 (OBJECT)

        Because its grammar depends on the word order, Glosa works best if
we keep to the normal (S-V-O) structure in clauses.  In poetry and fiction,
however, the stylistic variations are necessary.

FUNCTION WORDS
        These are the small words that are not concept/words, but which
join these words together (conjunctions), lead us into a phrase or clause
(prepositions), create negatives, introduce questions, or modify the timing
of an action when placed before a `verb' (tense particles).  There are also
the small words placed before `nouns' to tell us that they are things, and
how many, or how much, of them there are (determinants).
        Joining words:  e, sed, pluso, alo, ni

        Prepositions: tem, a, ex, de, anti, seqe, po, pre, vice, krom ....

        Negatives: ne, nuli, no-, ni ... ni, nuli-

        Questions: Qe, qestio, qod, qo-

        Tense particles: pa, fu, du, nu, pra, sio, ge-, gene

        Before `nouns': u, plu, plura, uno, vario, poli, oligo, u mero de
         (determinant)

SENTENCES
        Putting all the above together, in Glosa, as in English, sentences
can be made up of various combinations of phrases and clauses - as long as
the Subject-Verb-Object rule is observed.  In Glosa, also, there is the
added requirement that words in phrases follow the rule of increasing
importance, with the main concept/word last.

        If the sentence is made up of phrases only, there is a single
`verb', and the sentence is described as a Major Clause.  If there are two
or more `verbs', then the `verb' describing the main action forms the Main
Clause, and the other clauses (NC, AdvC, AdjC) are called Subordinate
Clauses.  The whole sentence can, however, still be called the Major
Clause.
        The secret of good sentence formation is in using well-formed
phrases to make up the sentences and their constituent clauses.

PRECEDENCE IN PHRASES
RULE: In a phrase, a word is modified by the word in front of it (its
        precedent.)
RULE: In a normal sentence (Major Clause), there is a tendency for the
        first noun phrase (SUBJECT) to modify the verb phrase (VERB), and
        then, for the verb phrase to modify the second noun phrase (OBJECT).
RULE: A modifying clause (Adjectival or Adverbial) follows directly after
        the concept/word that it modifies; this structure is indicated
        by the placing of a semi-colon (;) or possibly a colon (:) before
        the new clause.

 In a noun phrase, the sequence is:-

           determinant  -  number  -  quantity  -  modifier(s)  -  noun

        e.g. plu tri no-ge-numera, fo sono, no-puri ju-an
             the three uncounted, shouting, dirty boys

           Note.  The concept/word used as a noun is the only element of
              a noun phrase that is essential.

 In a verb phrase, the sequence is:-
                                                    |   verb
  negative - tense - modifier(s) - auxiliary verb - |      OR
 particle                                           | verboid + amplifier

                     e.g. ne pa hedo, no-soni tenta | kursi
                                                    | ki ana

                       did not happily, quietly try | to run
                                                    | to go up

           Note, again.  The concept/word used as a verb is the only
               element of a verb phrase that is essential.

 %%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

_A second Seminar on Glosa____________________________R. Gaskell Sept,1998_
-----------------------------______________________------- File: SEMINAR.#2

BASIC GRAMMAR RULES
                     There are three basic rules behind the grammar of
Glosa: combined, they tell us 'position determines function'.

        1. S-V-O: Subject, Verb, Object order in a clause.
        2. "A word is modified by its precedent."  One word modifies the
             next, and so on.
        3. A word can function, within reason, as any part of speech.
             There are no Part-of-Speech markers, but a word's function is
             indicated by its position in a sentence.
        __________________________________________________________________
        All other rules of Glosa usage follow from these.
All sentences contain a 'verb': it is the word that tells us the action
occurring in the sentence; it gives the sentence _life_.

VERBS
        In Glosa, the word in the middle of the sentence, describing the
action, is the 'verb' ('verb' = word functioning as a verb).

  EG  U kani bibe u hidra.  ...................  bibe  'verb'
             ~~~~
   [The dog drinks the water.]

  EG  An fo hedo bibe.  .......................  hedo  'verb'
            ~~~~   ............................  bibe  'gerund' (verb/noun)
   [He very-much enjoys to drink/drinking.]

  EG  Mi don ad un equs id bibe.  .............  don(a)  'verb'
         ~~~        ...........................  bibe  'noun'
   [I give (to) the horse its drink.]

N.B. 'verb' =  word functioning as a verb
     'noun' =  word functioning as a noun
      ' X ' =  word functioning as an  X

"IS" IN ENGLISH
                 This word is used in many different ways in English, but,
in Glosa, the "is" concept is conveyed more accurately - using a range of
'verbs'.  As an 'action' its original function was to represent an "equals"
sign, EG "He is John."  Glosa translations for various uses of "is" in
English are:-

  es  -  to be, is (has equivalence with), has the essence of
  habe - has the property (quality) of
  gene - gets to be, becomes
  eqa - equals, measures

Examples:
    Fe es u ju-fe.
   [She is a girl.]
    An es u filosofi-pe.
   [He is a philosopher.]

    U meteo habe frigo.
   [The weather is cold/has coldness] [It is cold.]
    Mi habe fo lati.
   [I am very tall/have much tallness.]

    U meteo gene ma termo.
   [The weather is warmer/gets warmer/becomes more hot.]
    U ju-an gene fatiga.
   [The boy is tired/becomes tired.]
    Mi habe fo turba; e fu gene koleri, si tu ne sto dice.
   [I am very upset, and will be angry if you don't stop talking.]

    U temperatura eqa tri-ze grada (30`C).
   [The temperature is thirty degrees (30`C).]
    U preci de ki-in u teatra eqa mo-penta dolara ($15).
   [The price of entry into the theatre is fifteen dollars ($15).]

COMMON 'VERBS'
                In Glosa, some words are used almost exclusively as verbs,
and these are general-purpose verbs, such as:-

  habe - to have, have has
  ki  -  to go, go, goes, went
  veni - to come, come, comes, came
  tena - to hold, grab, take

VERBOIDS
          Some words, in Glosa, are "almost verbs" but are not "full verbs"
in their own right: they are not 'stand-alone' verbs, but they add to the
meaning of the "full verb", which is usually the final word in the Verb
Phrase.  These Verboids are half-way between Adverbs and Verbs.  Examples:-

  posi - possibly
  pote - can, has the ability to
  nece - needs to, must
  lice - be allowed, allow to
  debi - ought to
  hedo - enjoys, happily
  forti - strongly
  seqe - following, sebsequently
  vo(lu) - wish to, would
  tanto - so much, almost
  tosto - soon

EG  Mi fu posi ki ad u boteka.
   [I will possibly go to the shop.]
    An pote kurso fo celero.
   [He can run very quickly.]
    Kron mi voka, fe tosto veni in u domi.
   [When I call, she soon comes into the house.]
    Mi gina fu hedo kanta pro vi.
   [My wife will happily sing for you.]
    Tu lice habe id.
   [You are allowed to have it.]
    Pa noktu, mi tanto ariva pre u pluvi.  .. (Pastness implied by "Pa" so
   [Last night I almost arrived before the rain.]  'pa tanto ariva' wrong.)

AUXILIARY VERBS
                 These words 'help' the verb to do its job.  But they
usually follow the "main verb" as separate words, though they contribute to
the formation of a "compound verb."  Examples are:-

  ana - up   EG  ki ana  [ascend]
  kata - down   EG  ki kata  [descend]
  retro - back   EG  kurso retro  [run back]
  itera - again   EG  akti itera  [do (it) again; repeat]
  ab  -  away   EG  greso ab  [walk away]
  versi - back to start   EG  viagia versi  [travel home]
  avanti - forward   EG  ki avanti  [advance]

EG  An pa ki kata un orto-lo; sed an nece skende itera ana id, te
   [He fell down the cliff, but (he) must climb again up it, in order to

    gene an kefa-ve; qi veni ab an kefa pre an pa kade.
    get his hat, which came off his head before he fell.]

WAYS IN WHICH THE 'VERB' MAY BE MODIFIED
  In Glosa, the word functioning as the verb can be modified by placing
short words, called particles, before it.  These particles might alter the
time of the event, negate it, turn it back on the Subject, or make it
conditional on some other event.  As well as that, the action might act
upon - or not act upon - an Object; and then, for a change, the
positioning Subject and Object is sometimes switched around.

. TENSE PARTICLES
                   Dealt with, in detail - in the Tense Table - these short
words, which come at the start of a Verb Phrase, usually singly but
sometimes in a group, tell us WHEN an action occurs.

     - No particle before the 'verb' suggest a present tense (but can mean
        that an earlier time indication logically applies).
 pa  -  Simple Past Tense  (pa  [did])
 fu  -  Future Tense  (fu  [will])
 nu  -  An immediate Present Tense  (nu  [just now])
 du  -  Continuous Tense   (du X  [is X-ing, continues to X])
 pra -  Past Perfect Tense: fully completed.   (pra  [had])

  EG  Un andra nu pa veni in u domi.
               ~~~~~
     [The man just now came into the house.]

CONDITIONAL
             When an action happens only if something else occurs before
it, then we say that the second event is conditional on the first: in
English, this is usually shown by the use of "would", "should" or "could".

 sio  -  would
 sio debi - would ought, should
 sio posi - would possibly, could

  EG  Si mu gene pluvi, plu-ci sperma sio posi kresce in alti fito.
     [If they get rain, these plants could grow into tall bushes.]

NEGATIVE
          When an action does NOT happen, we indicate this using a negative
word before the verb.  This happens in English, and something very similar
occurs in Glosa.

 ne  -  does not
 no  -  un-
 nuli - never  (also used with 'nouns':  nuli ra  [no thing])
 ni X ni Y - neither X nor Y

  EG  U gina ne ki a plu boteka; ka fe vagona ne ergo.
     [The woman did not go to the shops, because her car did not work.]

REFLEXIVE
            When an action affects the person or thing that caused it, we
say that the action is a "reflexive" action.  In Glosa there are two
particles that are used after the 'verb' to indicate that the action is
received by the Subject of the sentence: one if the Subject is human, the
other for non-human Subjects:-

 -se  -  to itself, itself
 auto -  to himself/herself, himself/herself, themselves

  EG  Jon vagona frakto-se, tem an du age ad an ergo-lo.
     [Jon's car broke itself while he was driving to work (his work-place).]

      Kron fe skende kata plu grada; fe pa kade, e noku auto.
     [When she descended the stairs, she fell and hurt herself.]

TRANSITIVE/
        /INTRANSITIVE   While the standard English sentence, has two Noun
Phrases separated by a Verb Phrase, some sentences have no real Object -
receiver of the action: these are called "intransitive" sentences. Glosa
also follows this pattern:-

  Transitive  S-V-O   EG  U kani fago u karni.  [The dog eats the meat.]
  Intransitive  S-V   EG  U kani voka.    [the dog barks.]
  Intransitive  S-V(-IO)  EG   U kani voka ad u posta-pe.
   IO --> Indirect Object     [The dog barks at the post-man.]

PASSIVE
 VOICE   While most of English and Glosa has the normal structure where
         the thing doing the action comes first, and whatever receives the
action comes last (called Active Voice), both languages allow the reciever
of the action to get a mention at the start of the sentence (called Passive
Voice).  It can be thought of as an (O-) V (-S) sentence:-

 X V Y  -  X does action V to Y   EG  U kani morda un andra.
  .................................  [The dog bites the man.]

 Y gets V-ed by X  -  Y has action V done to it by X
  EG  Un andra gene ge-morda ex u kani.
     [The man gets bitten by the dog.]

To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] not all, but some letters.
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk>
Date: Sat, 3 Nov 2001 13:04:32 -0000

>> Alex wrote
>>> I think that we must use "plu" or "u" for the starting of noun-phrase.
>>> The three phrases in Russian are very similiar. They don't use any word
for
>>> meaning of article. And in glosa they should use only "u" for singular
and
>>> nothing else.
>>
>At 15:52 01/11/2001 -0800, Charles wrote:
>>I agree. U/PLU are just singular/plural markers, not really articles.
>>Asians will prefer to elide them in many cases. To express definiteness
>>requires different particles (determiners). Any determiner can serve
>>to mark the beginning of a noun phrase (mainly, the direct object).
>>
>Robin Gaskell wrote
>* Plu Amika,
>I agree that the meaning of "determinants" and the functions of "u" and
"plu" do not necessarily match up.
>However, because for me, an English-speaker, the "a" and "the" of English
are part of my thinking pattern, I have uncritically >accepted the Glosa
dictionary listing:-
>U = the, a
>PLU = the (plural)

And you are not wrong to do so. ~U~ and ~plu~ can almost always
be translated by 'a/the' and 'some/the' in English. But there 'real'
function
is a noun phrase markers. And they don't show the definite/indefinite
distinction English speakers are used to.

My contention is that this distinction is redundant. This may be trying
to prove a negative, which, I believe, is impossible. So I've been
attempting to get someone to provide an example that proves the
hypothesis wrong and that shows the distinction is significant.

Thanks for your interesting examples.

Note: The word list definitions -
~U(n)~ = a, an, one, the, any
~Plu~ = more than one

>Plu ju-an pa trans u via. ( a number of boys; one road )
>But, where a numerator is used, it is fairly obvious that either U or PLU
are not needed.
>Tri ju-an trans bi via. ( 3 boys; 2 roads )

Agreed.

>BUT Plu tri ju-an; qi habita u proxi viki,
>[the 3 boys who live-in the nearby village]
>pa trans plu bi via; alelo na viki.
>[did cross the 2 roads (which are) beside our village]

>Did I use ~plu~ as a "determinant" in both of these cases, or was I simply
trans-scribing my English usage into Glosa?
>If there are only three boys in the next village, these are they.
>If there is a particular pair of roads running beside our village, then
these are the same two roads.

Great example.
~Plu tri ju-an~ [More-than-one three boys]
Here ~plu~ doesn't map to 'the'. The English
phrase is definite because you know how
many boys live there. If you wish to wish to
communicate this fact then why not say,

~Panto tri ju-an; qi eko in certa proxi viki~
[All three boys, who live in a particular nearby village]

>Real problems used to present themselves for me in Glosa, when I
specifically wanted to use
>the "a" [one of] function, and also when, in English, I would naturally say
"some" [an amount of].

Interesting. Robin thinks of ~u~ as primarily meaning 'the',
and Charles (in another posting ) thinks of ~u~ (and ~mo~) as
meaning 'a'.

>Nowadays, I use ~uno~ [= one of the] to specify an un-named singularity.
>EG Uno andra pa kine-auto to un antero de u grega.
>[a man did move himself to(position) the front of the group]
>A man stepped to the front of the group.

Here the man is 'indefinite' in English because
he hasn't been mentioned before. In the next
sentence he will be 'definite'.

"The group became silent to listen to
what the man had to say."

I think this is clear from the context.
If it was a different man who was
being listened to, it could just be
~hetero andro~ instead of ~un andro~ .

>And for years I thought there could be a Glosa word, like ~des~ [of the] to
translate the general usage of "some" in English. >But, in trying to fit in
with the Ashford dictionaries, I decided that Glosa did, in fact have two
"somes"
>IE plura = several ...... for COUNTABLES, and
>mero de = part of .... for NON-COUNTABLES.
>EG Tem intra u kuko-ka, plura de plu gyna pa fago (u) mero de u kasea.
>[while in the kitchen several of the women ate a part of the cheese]
>While in the kitchen, some women ate some of the cheese.

I would say that ~plura~ is more specific than 'some',
meaning 'a small number of', and that 'some' is just
~plu~ [more than one] in the first usage.

'Some of the cheese' = ~ex kasea~
'A piece of cheese' = ~mero kasea~
'All the cheese'= ~holo kasea~

~U mero de u kasea~.
This seems to me to be an unnecessarily close copy
of the English 'a piece of the cheese'. (Note: one ~u~
is 'a' and one is 'the'). ~Mero kasea~ seems sufficient
to me.

>Well, this sort of word game keeps me happy ... and it might be good Glosa
as well.

Good Glosa is Glosa that communicates the intended meaning.

Poli saluta,
Nick

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Cheese! (was: not all, but some letters)
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 3 Nov 2001 08:43:24 -0800 (PST)

--- Nick Hempshall <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk> wrote:
> 'Some of the cheese' = ~ex kasea~
> 'A piece of cheese' = ~mero kasea~
> 'All the cheese'= ~holo kasea~
> 
> ~U mero de u kasea~.
> This seems to me to be an unnecessarily close copy
> of the English 'a piece of the cheese'. (Note: one ~u~
> is 'a' and one is 'the'). ~Mero kasea~ seems sufficient
> to me.

Also, ~ex kasea~ sounds like something popping out of the
cheese--like a child of the cheese--which is not something
I want on my dinner table. 

My list has ~mero~ as meaning "portion". So I guess I would
tend to use ~mero~ to refer to some portion or fraction of
the cheese, and ~blocka~ to refer to a piece of chese.

So I might use ~mero de kasea~ (portion of cheese), and
~kasea bloka~ ("cheese block"). I think the "de" is
important here, if you're going to put the adjective
(cheese) after the noun (portion, block). Just a guess on
my part.

I'm ok with some level of ambiguity here. English itself is
pretty vague when you refer to "some" or "a piece" of
cheese. Those could be slivers or huge piles. If you want
to be more clear, add more words: ~mega~ ~pusi~ ~proxi
semi~ ~bi kilograma~ etc.

Kevin

========
To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>, <kevins{at}pobox{dot}com>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] not all, but some letters.
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk>
Date: Sat, 3 Nov 2001 17:47:16 -0000

Kevin Smith  wrote:
> One feature I want to add to my word list is to indicate
> which words automatically begin a noun-phrase, and which
> automatically begin a verb-phrase. I'll be looking to y'all
> to help me figure those out.

I would like to raise the possibilty that the use of the terms 
noun-phrase and verb-phrase, is importing English grammatical
categories into Glosa, where they only approximately fit.

Similarly Latin grammatical terms and patterns where artificially
applied during the beginings of the study of English grammar,
because that's what people were used to thinking about.

Consider: ~Mi grafo poesi~.
~Mi~ is the first word, so the start of something.
But is it 'I' or 'My' or somewhere in between?
~Grafo~ is 'write', so ' I write' or 'My writing'.
And here we have already assumed that the default
tense is the present tense. It is more natural to speak
or write about something that has already happened, 
rather than to commentate on what's going on in the 
present moment. So 'I wrote' or 'My past writing'.
Or is the writer omitting the tense marker to suggest
a timeless fact, 'It is my custom to write' or 'Something
that is, was and will be characteristic of me, is my writting'?
~Poesi~ is 'poetry', and so also 'poetically'.
'How' I write or 'what' I write or somewhere in between.

Note: ~Mi ge-grafo poesi~ 'My written poetry'
~Mi poesi grafo~ 'I do-poetry in the form of writting'
               i.e. not oral poetry.       
or 'My poetry writing' (Answer to the question 'What do you
like best about yourself?')

So, what conclusions follow.

Pronouns can mark the beginning of a noun-phrase-equivalent,
or indicate the next word is the beginning of the verb-phrase-equivalent.
But the pronoun can be preceded by a 'quantifier'. 
So ~panto na~ 'all of us', ~tetra mu~ 'four of them'.

All verb-phrase-equivalents can start with ~ne~.
But ~Ne mi.~ 'Not me' (Answer to the question 'Who likes cabbage?')
Affirmative verb-phrase-equivalents can begin with a 'time marker',
~nu~, ~fu~ or ~pa~.
But  ~pa di~ 'yesterday', ~fu anua~ 'next year', ~nu di~ 'nowadays'.
The 'aspect marker' ~du~ can begin an affirmative 
verb-phrase-equivalents, if no 'time marker' is being used and
the action is 'progressive or continuous'.
{Is ~du~ considered to be a different word from ~dura~?}
Affirmative conditional verb-phrase-equivalents begin with ~sio~.
{Is there any other known use of ~sio~?}
Verb-phrase-equivalents can begin with 'helper verb'.
'Helper verbs' can also be full verbs, or nouns or adjectives or adverbs.

~U~ and ~plu~ would show that start of the noun-phrase-equivalent.
      ~Mi grafo u poesi~ (the stuff) compared 
      with ~Mi grafo ko poesi~ (the manner).
And there is another set of words that can be included here but which 
may have other uses also, ~oligo~, ~hetero~, ~pluso~, ~certa~, 
~plura~, ~poli~, ~mega~, ~sati~, ~panto~, ~holo~, ~mero~, ~mikro~,
~uno~, ~ali~, ~singu~, ~pusi~, ~solo~, ~speci~, ~nuli~, ~qo~.
And the ~u~/~plu~ compound words, ~u-ci~, ~u-la~, ~plu~ci~, ~plu-la~.
But these can also be 'pronouns'.
Numerals, ~mo~, ~bi~, etc can start the noun-phrase-equivalent.
But ~u bibli penta~ 'the fifth book'.

Conclusion: Either this is a lot more complicated than expected or
we are looking at it from the wrong point of view.

Saluta,
Nick

act as 'quantifier'. Compare 'enough cheese', 
'too much cheese', 'a lot of cheese'.

========
To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] not all, but some letters.
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk>
Date: Sat, 3 Nov 2001 20:27:33 -0000

Charles wrote
> No. Noun and verb phrases are absolute linguistic universals.

'Absolute' universals are very few and far between in linguistics.
For example - 'All languages have vowels'.

Noun and verb phrases are so called because the head of the
phrase is a noun or a verb. That raises the question as to what
is a noun or a verb. Harder to answer than might be thought.

It could be said that a noun is the name of something. But
is beauty a 'thing'? Isn't the adjective 'red' also the name
of a colour?

So definitions based on meaning have been replaced by
definitions based on structural features, that signal the 
way in which classes of words behave in a language.

Glosa's lack of inflexions and use of words in multiple
grammatical word classes, make this determination of
the class of the head word less than obvious, and allow
the possibility that Glosa can have phrases that are not
definitely noun or verb phrases.

Saluta,
Nick

After speaking/writing a few such sentences, you will certainly
drop it in favor of appropriate tense particles and auxiliaries.

I see no problem with the verbs as long as those aren't elided.
There is a similar problem with some people attempting to
forego any sign of the noun phrase -- it doesn't work.

Glosa is not unique. It is a sort of English-Chinese-Euro pidgin.
Pidgins/creoles have a known structure. This isn't quite like Lojban,
an unconstrained toy/experiment. It has to follow basic linguistic
principles and be a real human language.

Conlangers are free to go elsewhere (as I sometimes do myself)
and indulge their imaginations. Glosa is supposed to be serious.

And that's where I'm stumbling at the moment. Glosa seems
to be a bit too complex (or ambiguous, at the writer's
discretion) if you follow conventional rules and thought
patterns. 

Nick seems to be pointing out that Glosa may be suited to
thinking about such things in a different way, and at the
moment, I'm inclined to agree. 

Now whether Glosa can still be used effectively with a
conventional mindset is another question. And whether a new
mindset suited to Glosa is possible or desirable is yet
another question.

For now, I am certainly open to the possibiliy that Glosa
may currently be structured in a way that allows for (or
encourages?) phrases to be neither clearly noun-phrase, nor
clearly verb-phrase. 

Perhaps they must always be more one than the other. Or
perhaps there are ambiguities in the language that should
be "fixed". Or perhaps there are desireable ambiguities.
Or...?

I am not a linguist. I am enjoying learning about languages
and how they affect (and are affected by) our thought
patterns. I am mostly interested in finding, and using, a
simple and effective IAL, whether it follows universal
rules or breaks them.

Kevin

========
To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] not all, but some letters.
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk>
Date: Sun, 4 Nov 2001 08:10:01 -0000

Paul O. Bartlett wrote
>   Except that noun-phrase and verb-phrase structures, by whatever
> names and whatever structures, seem to be pretty much linguistic
> universals.  To be sure, in some languages (such as English to some
> extent) a given word may serve as a noun or verb in different contexts,
> but regardless those languages still have noun phrases and verb phrases.

Well, perhaps, I could put it another way.

~Mi grafo poesi~ can be analysed in 4 ways.

1. ~Grafo~ is VP and ~poesi~ is direct object NP.
2. ~Grafo~ is VP and ~poesi~ is adverb.
3. ~Grafo poesi~ is a compound VP, on the ~dice qestio~ [to ask] &
                 ~lose bio~ [to die] model.
4. ~Mi grafo poesi~ is NP and therefore a 'minor sentence' meaning
                 [My writing poetry (is an event that exists).]

My secondary point was that the omission of the 'time marker' 
could be intended to indicate that the 'event' is 'timeless' and
that it is, was and will be a characteristic of the speaker's
behaviour.

My tertiary point being that the ambiguity could be intentional,
but that Glosa has structures that can remove the ambiguity if
it is not intentional.

Saluta,
Nick

Saluta, Charles,

Saturday, November 03, 2001, 10:51:58 PM, tu pa grafo:

CCC> Definiteness is always expressible in a language. Articles are
CCC> just one way to do it. Context alone does not suffice.
Still, Russian does not use articles. And I couldn't say that Russian
is less precise than English, in general.

One example has come to my mind. It is concerned with general
obligidary grammar features, rather than definiteness itself.

In Russian, verbs in past tense are inflected on gender. Thus, when I
hear a sentence 'Ya shla domoy' (= 'I went home'), I know, that the
speaker is a woman ('shla' is feminine singular past form). The phrase
'Ya shyol domoy' means the same ('I went home'), but says us that the
speaker is male. I doubt that such information can be expressed in
English in some normal form (that is, in a form which can be found in
real speach). Does this mean that English is a defective, non-complete
language?

On the other hand, in English, nouns (or, precisely, noun phrases),
have obligidary definiteness markers, which are absent in Russian. And
the differences betweeen 'Washington is a capital' and 'Washington is
the capital' dissappear in Russian translation...

Sorry, if you cannot catch what I mean. I couldn't express my thoughts
more clear :(((

> Still, Russian does not use articles. And I couldn't say that Russian

I believe that all natural languages can (and must) express
all the same things. Over time, they all have converged to a
similar level of expressiveness balanced with difficulty.
(But an artificial language can be designed to be easier
to learn by adults as a second language.) I'm sure Russian
has ways to express definiteness without articles, just as
English can express gender without inflections, and like
Chinese expresses number without our style of plurals.

> In Russian, verbs in past tense are inflected on gender. Thus, when I
> hear a sentence 'Ya shla domoy' (= 'I went home'), I know, that the
> speaker is a woman ('shla' is feminine singular past form).

I guess that Russian would use "one" or "some" to indicate
indefiniteness when necessary?

> On the other hand, in English, nouns (or, precisely, noun phrases),
> have obligidary definiteness markers, which are absent in Russian. And
> the differences betweeen 'Washington is a capital' and 'Washington is
> the capital' dissappear in Russian translation...

Many of the English uses for articles can be elided without problems.
My example illustrated the difference between "X is one of the Y"
and "X is the only Y", which must be easy to express in any language.

> Sorry, if you cannot catch what I mean. I couldn't express my thoughts
> more clear :(((

Perhaps, you are right, and I just don't see those ways.
Of course, we have construction like 'That very X, who ...'. But in
other cases we normally don't use any words to express
definiteness/indefiniteness.
I would like to study this question. I suggest that you give me a
number of sentences, in which definiteness is very significant. And I
will tell you how they are translated in Russian...

CCC> I guess that Russian would use "one" or "some" to indicate
CCC> indefiniteness when necessary?
We can say 'somebody', but it's third person. In 1st and 2nd persons
gender specification is obligidary and cannot be skipped.

CCC> Many of the English uses for articles can be elided without problems.
CCC> My example illustrated the difference between "X is one of the Y"
CCC> and "X is the only Y", which must be easy to express in any language.
In Russian these two sentences would be normally translated the same
way. Say, 'George Bush is a President' is just 'George Bush -
President' (copula is omitted in usual speach). 'George Bush is the
President of the United States' is 'George Bush - prezident
Soyedinennyh Shtatov' (look at the absence of any determinator before
'Soyedinennyh Shtatov' = 'the United States').

>> Sorry, if you cannot catch what I mean. I couldn't express my thoughts
>> more clear :(((

CCC> You write in English very well and clearly.
Thanks. I had problems not only with my English, but with putting my
thought in words, in general.

========
To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] Word Classes
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk>
Date: Mon, 5 Nov 2001 22:09:54 -0000

I wrote
> That raises the question as to what is a noun or a verb. 
> 
> It could be said that a noun is the name of something. But
> is beauty a 'thing'? Isn't the adjective 'red' also the name
> of a colour?
> 
> So definitions based on meaning have been replaced by
> definitions based on structural features, that signal the 
> way in which classes of words behave in a language.
> 
> Glosa's lack of inflexions make this determination of
> the class of the head word less than obvious.

Charles pointed out that inflexions have been replaced 
by pre-positioned particles in Glosa. Whilst these are
more likely to be omitted, than are inflexions, in texts, 
they do give a route to a definition of word class based 
on a word's relationship to them.

For example one could start to define 'nouns' as any word that
occurs as a head word in a phrase beginning ~u~ or ~plu~.

~u mega tabla~ [table]
~u bibli tri~       [book]
~plu poda de u zoa~   [leg] [animal] etc

But then one might meet with

~u vora~     [meal/eating]
~u kanta~   [song/singing]  etc

Are these words in the same word class?
Would one need to restrict the 'noun' class to words that
*don't* occur as head words in phrases being ~du~?

How about ~U ju-an du pa nasa u kuko.~?

What class would ~nasa~ be? Is true to say it's a noun 
being used as a verb? Or should be there be a separate 
class of words that can do this? Are the following
sentences correct?

~U nasa de flori es gluko.~    [smelling a flower is nice]
~U nasa de andro es rubi.~    [the man's nose is red]

Poli ami saluta,
Nick

PS I've now been reminded that ~plu~ occurs after 
the possessive pronoun in standard Glosa texts, so it is not 
always guaranteed to be the start of a noun-phrase-equivalent.
Exempla - ~mi plu bibli~ [my books]

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Word Classes
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Mon,  5 Nov 2001 15:28:08 -0800

Nick wrote:

> ~u vora~     [meal/eating]
> ~u kanta~   [song/singing]  etc
>
> Are these words in the same word class?

ISTM that there are only two word classes in True Glosa,
particles and the content words. This corresponds to the
Chinese distinction between "empty" and "full" words.
Similar to linguistic terminology that categorizes words
by degree of "semantic bleaching", i.e. particles are
words that have had most of their meaning bleached out
so they can perform syntactic/grammatical functions.

What makes VORA a "verb" is placing it in a verb phrase.
In a noun phrase it becomes a noun. The terms "noun" and
"verb" are dispensible in teaching Glosa, which always
seems to please grammar-hating students. Just show them
a few sentence patterns, and they get the idea. Anyway,
that was the design goal of the authors.

So, rather than sub-classing the concept words, we should
be looking at the noun-forming and verb-forming particles
and arranging them into a neat table or list of lists.

NF: u plu mi de ...

VF: pa es sti ...

--- Nick Hempshall <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk> wrote:

> that occurs as a head word in a phrase beginning ~u~ or

As Charles pointed out (more or less), a Glosa word may be
a 'noun' in one sentence, and a 'verb' in another. Words
represent concepts, and may be used as verbs, nouns,
adjectives, or adverbs, as long as the meaning makes sense.

Mi du nasa per mi nasa. Mi nasa pedi du algo. An pa nasa
dice. (He spoke nasally?)

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] not all, but some letters.
From: charles{at}catty{dot}com
Date: Mon,  5 Nov 2001 20:30:40 -0800

> I would like to study this question. I suggest that you give me a
> number of sentences, in which definiteness is very significant. And I
> will tell you how they are translated in Russian...

This is a fair question, so I must take a swing at it:
Imagine we are walking around the lake, you and me (both male)
and Jan (a female), when suddenly we encounter a pack of coyotes.
We agree to use the word "KANI" for "coyotes". Usually I just
chase them away, but tonight we will practice Glosa instead.

Jan:  "Plu mega kani!"
C:    Yes, I think the plural marker should precede the adjective.
P:    Why waste time with articles? Reduplication is better here.
Jan:  "Kani! Kani! ... Mega kaniiiiiiiii!"
Kani: "Boni gusta, u gina."
N:    See, context is sufficient! But ambiguous too! Cool!
Kani: But I want to specify whether any or just this one is "boni".
M:    No problem. We can eliminate article "u" 3 lines above.
C:    "Boni gusta, gina" sounds like a command to the victim.
Syd:  We can use a preposition to separate it from the verb.
Jan:  You mean like "Boni gusta de gina"? That's descriptive ...
Kani: No. I definitely meant "Gina pa gusta boni ad mi".
C:    We need to try a less treacherous verb than "gusta".
Jan:  "Bate kani."
P:    There, that's perfect! No extraneous particles.
C:    Which one? "U maxi mega kani? Fe kani? Tri kani?"
N:    All of them, "Panto!"
Jan:  Excessive, I love my pet dog at home. "Bate justi kani-co."
C:    That's cheating, Glosa has no morphology.

Sorry. That's what happens every time I try to write in Glosa.
I think there are many different cases where we need specific
"determiners", and I'd prefer a strictly defined closed set
of them, clearly described with examples of each. But it's
not so easy to do that.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re[2]: [glosalist] Word Classes
From: Mikhail Adigeyev <madigeyev{at}yandex{dot}ru>
Date: Tue, 6 Nov 2001 10:02:44 +0300

Kevin pa grafo tem 2001-11-06:

KS> --- Nick Hempshall <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk> wrote:
>> For example one could start to define 'nouns' as any word
>> that occurs as a head word in a phrase beginning ~u~ or
KS> ~plu~.

KS> As Charles pointed out (more or less), a Glosa word may be
KS> a 'noun' in one sentence, and a 'verb' in another. Words
KS> represent concepts, and may be used as verbs, nouns,
KS> adjectives, or adverbs, as long as the meaning makes sense.

KS> Mi du nasa per mi nasa. Mi nasa pedi du algo. An pa nasa
KS> dice. (He spoke nasally?)
But I see a problem here, in words representing just concepts, without
determining them as basically 'nouns' or 'verbs'. Because nouns and
verbs are different mental entities, and mixing them is a kind of
trick, IMHO...
Let's take Nikc's example:
NH> ~u vora~     [meal/eating]
The basic meaning of the concept is 'eating', 'to eat' (act). When
using it as a noun, it should be naturaly mean 'the act/process of
eating'. But in one text about Glosa (can't recall which) I've met the
sentence 'Mi vora u vora', where 'u vora' mean 'food' (object of
eating), not the act. I beleave that 'eating' and 'food' are
_different_ concepts, and my intrinsic feeling of language says me
that they belong to different classes...

The things are even more complex, when we take words, which are
'basically nouns'. If we start from 'food' = (say) 'sito'. Is it a
noun or verb? Whan does the phrase 'Mi du sito' mean??? Does it mean
'I am cooking' or 'I am eaing', or 'I am feeding (somebody)? Or 'I am
being a piece of food'? :)))

Another example, which comes to my mind, is 'manu' = 'hand'. What
should it mean when used as a verb? The dictionary interprets it as
'hand over, pass, manage'. Why not 'to beat'? Or 'to gesticulate'?

I mean that for many (most?) words which are 'basically nouns', there
is no 'normal' way of interpreting them as verbs.

KS> Kevin


Saluta,
Mikhail
madigeyev{at}yandex{dot}ru

Mikhail wrote:

> Well, any language has indicative particle... That's the way both
> Russian and English do. In English I'd say 'this woman' (without
> article). In Glosa we can say 'u-ci gina', can't we?

Yes, and I think U-CI is probably the best answer for this.
Or maybe use just "CI GINA" if number is not relevant?

> I think this can be voluntary, used only when it's needed.  And in
> most cases some more specific words could play their (articles') role
> better.

Yes, I agree, I like non-mandatory particles for all these things.
When any one of them appears, it marks the beginning of a noun phrase.
Otherwise prepositions can do that job. Actually, the only problem is
the accusative, because all other cases are marked by prepositions,
and we can use "de" (or "em" or something else) for that.
But I still think Glosa needs better definiteness particles.

From: Mikhail Adigeyev <madigeyev{at}yandex{dot}ru>

Saluta, Glosa-pe!

I've almost finished my translating short info about Glosa into
Russian. I have one problem and would like to consult with y'all.

What is the precise difference in meaning between

It got broken. = Id pa gene frakti.

and

It is broken. = Id es ge-frakti.

?

BTW, I found some othe features which look odd for me.
Is Glosa's 'gene' a complete equivalent of English 'get'? For me
merging both 'to become' and 'to obtain' in one word is rather
illogical... For Russian student it would be rather difficult to
understand why phrases 'Id gene frakti' and 'Mi gene u feli' use the
same verb...

Some other features came to Glosa directly from English, I belive.
Say, in Russian weeks is suuposed to start from Monday, so calling
Mondi 'di-bi' (second day) is rather odd... (Though I dont know how it
can be fixed. Perhaps, using names for days of week would be more
convenient).

Another strange thing is 'Nona minuta po tri horo'. I think, that
Russian-style construction '3 horo 9 minuta' is more logical (we dont
call 6th of November '6 days post October' :))))

Saluta,
Mikhail

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au, Mikhail Adigeyev <madigeyev{at}yandex{dot}ru>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] got broken / is broken
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Tue, 6 Nov 2001 06:46:26 -0800 (PST)

--- Mikhail Adigeyev <madigeyev{at}yandex{dot}ru> wrote:
> What is the precise difference in meaning between
> 
> It got broken. = Id pa gene frakti.
>    and
> It is broken. = Id es ge-frakti.

I'll try. It will be interesting to hear other ideas.

For me, "it got broken" means that there was an action
sometime in the past that caused it to move from a
non-broken state to a broken state. "It is broken" merely
says that is is currently in a broken state, with no
indication of how. It might have been built broken, or just
deteriorated over time.

> BTW, I found some othe features which look odd for me.
> Is Glosa's 'gene' a complete equivalent of English 'get'?
> For me merging both 'to become' and 'to obtain' in one
word is
> rather illogical... For Russian student it would be
rather
> difficult to understand why phrases 'Id gene frakti' and 
> 'Mi gene u feli' use the same verb...

I agree. This is one of many places where Glosa has
inherited English idioms (or at least English thought
patterns). I don't know if it's worth changing, but it's
worth considering.

Since ge- is so commonly used, if it were to change I would
hope that gene would have to retain its "become" meaning,
and lose its "obtain" meaning. But we don't really have an
alternative already in the language.

On the other hand, we have the basic word ~esce~ which does
mean become, so ~gene~ could mean obtain, ~esce~ could be
become, and we could keep ~ge-~ as a legacy word no longer
directly derived from gene.

> Some other features came to Glosa directly from English,
> I belive. Say, in Russian weeks is suuposed to start from

> Monday, so calling Mondi 'di-bi' (second day) is rather
odd... 

This is a common problem in IAL's. Some start with Sunday,
others with Monday, and others use names (usually
Romance-derived). None of the solutions are great. Although
I prefer Monday = 1, I wouldn't change Glosa at this point.

> Another strange thing is 'Nona minuta po tri horo'. I
> think, that Russian-style construction '3 horo 9 minuta'
is more
> logical (we dont call 6th of November '6 days post
October' :))))

English allows either form.

Very interesting comments. Thanks!

Kevin

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] not all, but some letters.
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Tue,  6 Nov 2001 08:48:59 -0800

Mikhail wrote:

> Well, I prefer case markers myself. But this contradicts one of the
> main Glosa's principle (relying on strict word order in a sentence).

No contradiction, the order within a noun phrase is fixed:
case (ad, de, etc), possessives (mi, tu, etc), deictic (ci, co, etc),
number (u, plu, etc), gender (an, fe, etc), other adjectives, noun,
and then those horrible suffixes (-bo, -pe, etc).

> If a reform of Glosa would be done, I would definitely vote for
> case/slot markers :) They could play role of default
> noun-phrase-markers, by the way.
> Say, we use 'sa' and 'em' as subject and object markers respectively

We have a "subject" (really "agentive" or "ergative" or "ablative"): PER.
And for accusative object, either add EM or just use DE. This is
not really reform, just re-analysis.

> P.S. I don't think that such a reform if _necessary_, anyway. As a
> software developer, I know that there is a moment when we must freeze
> the project and stop chaotical 'improvements' ...
> P.P.S. But personally for me, some changes would definitely improve
> Glosa :)

If it isn't broken, don't fix it. Glosa is broken in a several places.
But its syntax is workable. Just adding a few missing particles would be
a very minor "reform". Fixing the main vocabulary, though, that is major.

From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com

Mikhail pa grafo:

>
> It got broken. = Id pa gene frakti.

It stays in a state of breakage.  <---- static state

> BTW, I found some othe features which look odd for me.
> Is Glosa's 'gene' a complete equivalent of English 'get'? For me
> merging both 'to become' and 'to obtain' in one word is rather
> illogical... For Russian student it would be rather difficult to
> understand why phrases 'Id gene frakti' and 'Mi gene u feli' use the
> same verb...

Good point. So use two different (auxiliary) verbs.
But actually Glosa has both GE and GENE for English GET ...
GE is auxiliary "become", but GENE seems more like "obtain".

Mi pa gene tri feli. (obtain)
Id (pa) ge frakti. (become)
Id es (ge) frakti. (state)

You didn't ask the harder question about "fall" vs. "drop".

Saluta, Charles,


CCC> We have a "subject" (really "agentive" or "ergative" or "ablative"): PER.
For me, PER corresponds to _instrumental_ case. If we use it to mark
nominative, we wouldn't say sentences like 'Mi bate u kuni per mi
manu'...
Also, Glosa is based on nominative/accusative case system, rather than
ergative/absolutive. Am I wrong? I don't think we could use PER to
mark subject in intransitive sentences ('Mi ki a loka')...

Mikhail

interesting and more logical :))

> Are there any online resources available, which contain wider info

Rick Harrison scanned in several pages, but just checking at
his web pages I couldn't find them anymore. You might write
to him and ask. I found it all very disappointing in detail.
But the basic idea of English-Chinese word order is still good.

You should also look into the Lingua Franca, a real pidgin
that served Europe for over 800 years; it has nearly the
same design.

Hogben's biggest mistake was using Greek vocab, I think.

proxi tri penta anua retro, mi pa tenta doci engelingua a u ju-an, dubaro 
nio, ex benglalanda (25n93e).an pa sedi in u-mi doci grupo e pa feno logi no 
ra. u hetera ju-an sed ex punjabilanda (35n75e) pa dice a mi ke dubar ( plu 
nima es no veri) pa dice gujerati-lingua. mi pa ki a u bibli-do e, po ma 
cerka, pa detekti u fo paleo bibli ko plu de u-co lingua, mi pa monstra id a 
dubar qi skope ad id ko u fo vaku facia. kusdev, de punjabi-landa, e mi pa 
lento seismo plu-na kefa - no veri. 
"urdu", kusdev pa ofere. bengla-landa pa es a u-la tem, est pakistan e u 
lingua de pakistan pa es urdu. u urdu bibli pa es ma facile de detekte so mi 
pa gene sko u urdu frase. "dubar, e kja cis he?", kusdev salta ana e kata ko 
hedo " kursi he. kursi he!" - nuli signi ex dubar!. an pa no dice urdu.
( na pa dice:- "u-ci ra es qo?", "es sedi-mobile") 
versi a bibli-do!. apo longi tem, mi detekti u mega e paki bibli ko multi 
lingua in id qi mi pa sume ab. 
kusdev, dubar e mi pa sti a pagina mo e na tri pa skope u lingua epi id, na 
pa skope pagina tri e ma e ma pagina. mi pa pote logi plu de pagina e kusdev 
plu hetera, punjabi-lingua e hindi-lingua e po multi pagina, dubar pa dice ko 
hedo "bengla" e sti lekto id. an pa pote lekti id facile e tako. po u tem, an 
pa feno enigma, an pa lekto u kristi bibli e an pa es u muslim. 
retro a bibli-do, id pa es dificile te gene u bibli de bengla-lingua. anti-co 
na pa profito e plu sko de enge-lingua pro dubar pa sti. sed. dubar pa no es 
u boni gene ski pe e u-an progresi pa es fo fo lento. mo di, mo de u 
engelanda ju-an qi pa habe simpati ko u-mi auxi dubar, pa dice ke mi pa es 
disipa u-mi tem e fo-tenta. u hetera ju-an ridi e dice ke mi debi audi u 
enge-lingua de dubar epi u bus!
proxi mo penta di seqe, mi pa akuti id!
u mi moto-me ruptu e mi nece ki a e ab u-mi sko-do per uti bi bus e mo di mi 
pa sume u mo qi pa ki ab mo mero de sko-do ad hetero proxi tri kilometri 
dista e dia ad u centra de civita. mi pa sto u bus, pa gradu epi id e pa 
skende plu skala. un akro tegu pa habe tri matura-an e un amaso de ju-an de 
u-mi sko-do.
u kako foni pa posi face pe ple non-audi, il pa es plu sporta milita, plu 
misili pa vola in pan via, plu bibli, plu saka, plu papira, plu stilo, plu 
pedi-ve, plu sito etc. u fenestra pa es aperi e em ma misili pa ge-jeti dia 
id e kata epi u movimento infra. .....................

On Tue, 6 Nov 2001, Mikhail Adigeyev wrote:

> I've met a number of mentions about Hogben's Interglossa. As far as I
> could understand, it had many interesting features, some of which are
> similar to those of my own toy conlang.
> 
> Are there any online resources available, which contain wider info
> about Interglossa's grammar?

    I have never been aware of much actual material online abut
Interglossa other than references.  Somewhere, if my memory serves
correctly, I read that the late Ron Clark actually acquired the
copyright to the book "Interglossa."  Occasionally used copies are
available from used book dealers.  I located one through the Advanced
Book Exchange, which is a valuable resources for finding used books in
English: http://www.abebooks.com .  There may also be another resource
at http://www.bibliofind.com , although I have never used it.

    Opinions vary about Interglossa.  Having looked at it, in some ways
I like it better than Glosa.  Charles has expressed his opinion that
Hogben's use of a predominantly Greek vocabulary was a mistake.  I
simply disagree, and neither of us is likely to convince the other. 
Interglossa came out during World War II and never really got a good
trial.  Sorry, I would say, although I doubt that Interglossa is likely
to get much of a trial at this late date.

========
To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Word Classes
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk>
Date: Tue, 6 Nov 2001 23:53:51 -0000

Mikhail Adigeyev wrote

> But I see a problem here, in words representing just concepts, without
> determining them as basically 'nouns' or 'verbs'. Because nouns and
> verbs are different mental entities, and mixing them is a kind of
> trick, IMHO...
> Let's take Nikc's example:
> NH> ~u vora~     [meal/eating]
> The basic meaning of the concept is 'eating', 'to eat' (act). When
> using it as a noun, it should be naturaly mean 'the act/process of
> eating'. But in one text about Glosa (can't recall which) I've met the
> sentence 'Mi vora u vora', where 'u vora' mean 'food' (object of
> eating), not the act. I beleave that 'eating' and 'food' are
> _different_ concepts, and my intrinsic feeling of language says me
> that they belong to different classes...

Yes. This is copying the English usage. 'I eat a meal'. While a meal
is an act-of-eating, you can't really eat the act. There is a tendency
in natural languages for the names for actions (-tion words in English)
to become 'concrete' nouns, for example, publication, the act of
publishing, becomes, the thing published, a magazine,etc.

In Glosa, the action can be made concrete by using an appropriate
'generic', for example, ~vora-ma~.

> The things are even more complex, when we take words, which are
> 'basically nouns'. If we start from 'food' = (say) 'sito'. Is it a
> noun or verb? Whan does the phrase 'Mi du sito' mean??? Does it mean
> 'I am cooking' or 'I am eaing', or 'I am feeding (somebody)? Or 'I am
> being a piece of food'? :)))

Primarily 'to feed (somebody)'.
But it's back to looking at the context again here. Or being more explicit
~Mi du dona sito (a pe)~  [I'm feeding (somebody)]
~Mi du gene sito~ [I'm eating]
~Mi du prepara sito~ [I'm cooking]

> Another example, which comes to my mind, is 'manu' = 'hand'. What
> should it mean when used as a verb? The dictionary interprets it as
> 'hand over, pass, manage'. Why not 'to beat'? Or 'to gesticulate'?

The verb is basicly ~akti per manu~, and so allowing lots of possible
meanings, and also implicitly saying that the 'noun' meaning is the primary
one. ~Manu~ = 'to manage' seems to have got in there on etymological
grounds, and I would not want to keep this meaning. ~Kontrola~ seems
better for 'to manage'

> I mean that for many (most?) words which are 'basically nouns', there
> is no 'normal' way of interpreting them as verbs.

There are some general interpretations
1) to use as a tool  ~akti per~
        hammer/to hammer         ~malea~
        telephone/to telephone    ~telefono~
2) to use a means of transport  ~ki per~
        train/to go by train           ~trena~
        car/to drive (go by car)   ~vagona~
2) to provide with  ~dona~
         frame/to frame (a picture)  ~qadra~
         colour/to paint                   ~kroma~


An early Glosa word list (1984) divides 'content' words into
'Things' (box,cake,fish,page,star,owe,death,victory, etc) and
'Concepts' (buy,flood,plough,sweat,funny,busy,green,too,sleep, etc),
with 'concepts' being usable as nouns, verbs and adjectives. Not
sure that is helpful. Perhaps that's why it was abandoned!

Saluta,
Nick

To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>, <kevins{at}pobox{dot}com>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] got broken / is broken
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk>
Date: Wed, 7 Nov 2001 00:25:13 -0000


> --- Mikhail Adigeyev <madigeyev{at}yandex{dot}ru> wrote:
> > BTW, I found some othe features which look odd for me.
> > Is Glosa's 'gene' a complete equivalent of English 'get'?
> > For me merging both 'to become' and 'to obtain' in one
> > word is
> > rather illogical... For Russian student it would be
> > rather
> > difficult to understand why phrases 'Id gene frakti' and 
> > 'Mi gene u feli' use the same verb...
> 
Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com> wrote
> I agree. This is one of many places where Glosa has
> inherited English idioms (or at least English thought
> patterns). I don't know if it's worth changing, but it's
> worth considering.

~Gene~ is 'get' in the passive sense of receiving something.

~Mi pa gene u grama~ 
I got a letter. I received a letter. A letter was delivered to me.

~Mi pa gene frigo~
I got cold. I received coldness. Coldness was given to me.
I became cold.

~Mi poda pa gene frakti~
My leg got broken. My leg received an act of breaking.
My leg was the object of an act of breaking.
An act of breaking was applied to my leg.

Note: ~frakti~ = 'breaking' and ~ge-frakti~ = 'broken'

Saluta,
Nick

To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Interglossa
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk>
Date: Wed, 7 Nov 2001 00:49:14 -0000

Charles wrote
> I think of it as an unfinished project; Ron+Wendy did a fair job
> of revising it, but it still remains incomplete and should be
> majorly revised one more time. As far as vocabulary source,
> certainly Greek is the braver and more interesting choice,
> but Ron decided to include Latin as well. I'd prefer it to
> draw from either Greek or Latin, but not both.

Actually, Hogben also used both Greek and Latin words in
Interglossa, extracting them from the elements that make 
up the scientific terminology.

Glosa's vocabulary is very similar to Interglossa. Ron and 
Wendy put in some shorter words as replacements for
some of Hogben's choices, for example, 'postula' (still in 
Glosa 1000) was replaced by 'si' as word for 'if'. Hogben 
thought that longer words give more time to think, and lead 
to better expression of one's ideas and less semantic change.

Saluta,
Nick

From: Mikhail Adigeyev <madigeyev{at}yandex{dot}ru>

Saluta, Nick,

Wednesday, November 07, 2001, 2:53:51 AM, tu pa grafo:


NH> In Glosa, the action can be made concrete by using an appropriate
NH> 'generic', for example, ~vora-ma~.
Thanks for explanations.

>> noun or verb? Whan does the phrase 'Mi du sito' mean??? Does it mean
>> 'I am cooking' or 'I am eaing', or 'I am feeding (somebody)? Or 'I am
>> being a piece of food'? :)))

NH> Primarily 'to feed (somebody)'.
NH> But it's back to looking at the context again here. Or being more explicit
NH> ~Mi du dona sito (a pe)~  [I'm feeding (somebody)]
NH> ~Mi du gene sito~ [I'm eating]
NH> ~Mi du prepara sito~ [I'm cooking]
If so, I think it's better to treat 'sito' as a noun, and use the
above constructions to make a verb. Using just 'du sito' can lead to
confusing...

>> I mean that for many (most?) words which are 'basically nouns', there
>> is no 'normal' way of interpreting them as verbs.

NH> There are some general interpretations
NH> 1) to use as a tool  ~akti per~
NH>         hammer/to hammer         ~malea~
NH>         telephone/to telephone    ~telefono~
NH> 2) to use a means of transport  ~ki per~
NH>         train/to go by train           ~trena~
NH>         car/to drive (go by car)   ~vagona~
NH> 2) to provide with  ~dona~
NH>          frame/to frame (a picture)  ~qadra~
NH>          colour/to paint                   ~kroma~
I'd unite the former two into one class - 'to use'
The last would include also cases like 'to turn into'.
I see one more variant - 'to be/become'...

Since there several ways of interpreting a noun as a verb, I'd prefer
use special 'light' or auxilary verb to 'verbify' (is it a term?) a
noun...

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] not all, but some letters.
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Wed,  7 Nov 2001 09:51:44 -0800

I wrote:

>> We have a "subject" (really "agentive" or "ergative" or "ablative"): PER.

Then, Mikhail wrote:

> For me, PER corresponds to _instrumental_ case. If we use it to mark
> nominative, we wouldn't say sentences like 'Mi bate u kuni per mi
> manu'...
>
> Also, Glosa is based on nominative/accusative case system, rather than
> ergative/absolutive. Am I wrong? I don't think we could use PER to
> mark subject in intransitive sentences ('Mi ki a loka')...

> P.S. For myself, I think that ergative/absolutive case system is more
> interesting and more logical :))

The most common pattern in language is to use topic-comment order
and a nominative alignment, so Glosa might as well do it that way.
What we usually call "subject" may be either the doer or receiver
of an action, and comes first in the sentence, and has no zero
case marking (i.e. no "preposition"). So the only way to indicate
a difference logically is to mark the verb as "active" vs. "passive".

The dog bites.   The man is bitten.
Kani morda.      Andro ge morda.

The dog bites the man.   The man is bitten by the dog.
Kani morda (de) andro.   Andro ge morda per kani.

For transitive sentences the "object" follows the verb and is
zero-marked for accusative, else has a preposition to show case.
This shows "agentive instrumental" is equivalent to "ergative".
And if an accusative marker is wanted, "de" works nicely.
Glosa's nominative and accusative are both normally zero-marked,
consistent with its tendency to elide particles when possible,
but they are available when necessary.

From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com

> Actually, Hogben also used both Greek and Latin words in
> Interglossa, extracting them from the elements that make
> up the scientific terminology.

That was the nose of the camel under the tent.

It used to be popular to appeal to "international scientific
vocabulary" but with today's poor educational system, that
fashion has receded. There aren't enough elements among the
"scientific combining forms" to make an IAL.

> Hogben thought that longer words give more time to think, and lead
> to better expression of one's ideas and less semantic change.

Yes, he had a lot of silly ideas like that. As I said, Ron
and Wendy improved it greatly, but it needs a version 3.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] word classes
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Wed,  7 Nov 2001 10:26:58 -0800

> I'd prefer use special 'light' or auxilary verb to 'verbify' (is it a term?) a noun...

I agree. Light verbs are the best way to design an IAL.
It minimizes the number of lexical items to be learned and
gives the users maximum leverage and power. It is possible
to analyze any English verb into a light verb construction.
I've posted several relevant URLs before ...

The truly important word classes are not the classic Latin ones,
but more like these:

head word (the bulk of the vocabulary)
particle (implementers of syntax)

Particles should be subclassed, like:

tense/aspect/mood: pa, du, ...
auxiliary verbs: es, ge, pote, ...
light verbs: face, moti, ...
(etc)

case prepositions: de, ad, per, ...
determiners: mi, u, u-la, ...
numbers: mo, panto, nuli, ...
(etc)

Ideally, all the particles can be arranged in a neat table
that displays their proper syntactic order and automatically
guides a learner in constructing sentences. I made one in
HTML with pull-down boxes once, then tried to sub-class
the main vocabulary items too, which would make a sort of
online interactive dictionary. Too much work ...

To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>, "Mikhail Adigeyev" <madigeyev{at}yandex{dot}ru>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] not all, but some letters.
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk>
Date: Wed, 7 Nov 2001 22:26:39 -0000

Mikhail Adigeyev wrote

> CCC> We have a "subject" (really "agentive" or "ergative" or "ablative"):
PER.
> For me, PER corresponds to _instrumental_ case. If we use it to mark
> nominative, we wouldn't say sentences like 'Mi bate u kuni per mi
> manu'...

Glosa uses ~per~ to mark the instrument and ~ex~ to mark the agent
in the case of a passive participle .

~U drama ge-grafo ex William Shakespeare~ [A play written by William
Shakespeare]
~Mu pa face musika per piano~  [They made music by-means-of a piano]
~An pa veni a Paris per trena~  [He came to Paris by train]


Saluta,
Nick

========
To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>, "Mikhail Adigeyev" <madigeyev{at}yandex{dot}ru>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Word Classes
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk>
Date: Wed, 7 Nov 2001 22:53:24 -0000

Mikhail Adigeyev wrote
> Since there several ways of interpreting a noun as a verb, I'd prefer
> use special 'light' or auxilary verb to 'verbify' (is it a term?) a
> noun...

These are what Hogben termed 'verboids'. They remain a feature of
Glosa, although, as one doesn't have to appear in very clause as it did
in Interglossa, they are less noticeable.

Quoting from "Central Glosa"

20 VERBIODS

These are 20 very interesting verbs, because, amazingly they play a
part in the action of all the many thousands of other verbs. They
are very useful as they will provide precision when there is a possibility
of mis-understanding.

AKTI             act, do, perform
AKUSTI        hear, listen
APO              remove
BALI             throw, send
DONA          give
DETEKTI      find
DICE             say, speak tell
EQA              be a member of
ESTE             feel
FACE            make, build, construct
GENE            get, receive, acquire
HABE            have, possess
KI                  go, move itself
LOSE            lose
MOTI            move something
REAKTI        react, respond 
STI                stimulate, excite, switch on
TENA            hold, keep from change
TRAKTO       pull, drag
VIDE              see

Saluta,
Nick

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au

From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>

At 18:59 03/11/2001 -0800, Kevin Smith wrote:
>--- Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com wrote:
>>   .....
>> After speaking/writing a few such sentences, you will
>> certainly drop it in favor of appropriate tense particles
>> and auxiliaries.
>
>After writing fairly extensively in Glosa, I'm still using
>~du~. In fact, I'm using it more now than when I started.
>
*   My first reaction is recalling some Chinese learners of English, who
were always saying things like,
         "I going to the shop."
    However, since your first language, kevin, is English, this reasoning
can't be relevant.  I suspect that you have found a mental pathway which
suits you, and find it apt.
    But the spirit of the language is the avoidance of unnecessary words,
and in the case of the SIMPLE PRESENT tense, the bare word being used as a
verb is the clean form of the language.
         ~Mi ki ad u boteka.~      SIMPLE PRESENT
         ~Mi du ki ad u boteka.~   PRESENT CONTINUOUS

    Also, between these two forms, I feel there is a functional semantic
difference, EG
         ~Singu di-mo, mi ki ad u boteka.~    ETERNAL PRESENT
         ~Mi ki ad u boteka, kron id habe aperi.~  "     "
         [ ................  when it is open]
     cf  ~Mi fu ki ad u boteka, tem id aperi.~   SIMPLE FUTURE
         [ ................     when it opens] 

         ~Mi age u vagona longi u via, e mi du ki ad u boteka.~
     OR
         ~Mi du age u vagona longi u via, e mi ki ad u boteka.~
     BUT NOT NECESSARILY
         ~Mi du age u vagona longi u via, e mi du ki ad u boteka.~
     NOR
         ~Mi age u vagona longi u via, e mi ki ad u boteka.~

   While I have no idea what the Linguists call this sort of intuitive use
of language, but I simply call it FEEL.

>> It has to follow basic linguistic
>> principles and be a real human language.
>
>I agree. A real _workable_, _usable_ language. I have tried
>to write quite a bit of original material and especially
>translated material in Glosa. I don't think anyone can
>judge  how effective Glosa is (or is not) unless/until they
>have done so.
>
*   This is good news - that you have done extensive wriiting.
    Which fairy tale are working on rendering into Glosa?  I found it
necessary to 'retell in Glosa' rather than to 'translate.'

Saluta, 

Robin Gaskell
