========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] not all, but some letters.
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Sat, 10 Nov 2001 02:54:18 +1100

At 19:05 03/11/2001 -0800, Kevin Smith wrote:
>--- "Paul O. Bartlett" <bartlett{at}smart{dot}net> wrote:
>> On Sat, 3 Nov 2001, Nick Hempshall wrote (excerpt):
>> 
>> > I would like to raise the possibilty that the use of
>> > the terms noun-phrase and verb-phrase, is importing 
>> > English grammatical categories into Glosa, where they 
>> > only approximately fit.
>> 
>>     Except that noun-phrase and verb-phrase structures,
>> by whatever names and whatever structures, seem to be 
>> pretty much linguistic universals.  
>
>And that's where I'm stumbling at the moment. Glosa seems
>to be a bit too complex (or ambiguous, at the writer's
>discretion) if you follow conventional rules and thought
>patterns. 
>
*   Well, if written badly, or hastily, Glosa can be more than ambiguous;
however, the intention of the authors was that the language, when used
well, is quite specific, and capable of only one interpretation - where
that was the writer's intention.

>Nick seems to be pointing out that Glosa may be suited to
>thinking about such things in a different way, and at the
>moment, I'm inclined to agree. 
>
*   The difference is a form of standardisation; thus Glosa is supposed to
use a sort of "universalised" syntax.  EG   to have , or to be.
       In French,   "L'eau habe froid."      =HAS (THE PROPERTY OF)
       In English,  "The water is cold."     =IS

    I believe that the French usage is the universalised form, and was
convinced of this by Ron Clark.

       In Glosa,   ~U hidra habe frigo.~  =HAS THE PROPERTY OF COLDNESS    

>Now whether Glosa can still be used effectively with a
>conventional mindset is another question. And whether a new
>mindset suited to Glosa is possible or desirable is yet
>another question.
>
*   Yes, these are three good questions.
   1. Conventional Mindset: I suspect that such a mind is set in the
conventions of its First Language; if people translate their national
idioms, metaphors, and natlang idiosyncratic syntax into Glosa, they are
likely to write Glosa which is hard to read for people from other
linguistic traditions.
   2. New mindset suited to Glosa: yes!  When you learn a new natlang, you
must try to get into the style of thinking of the native speakers of that
language.  This is also true of an auxlang, but it is a bit harder, because
there is no existing auxlang culture (of course the Espirantists would say
that one does exist), and an auxlang is supposed to be suitable for people
from all cultures to be able to wrap their minds around.  When a person can
confidently "think in Glosa" they have acquired the mindset suited to Glosa.
   3. A new minset suitable or possible: maybe not; possibly the world is
not ready for a concept-based language that uses raw syntax, without
morphological grammar .. now, or will be never ready .. ever.
      And maybe the hard-wired linguistic pathways that have evolved in the
human brain are not designed to express such 'raw thought.'
      However, when we put our mind to something, and if the gain is worth
it, we can step up to a higher level of mental functioning.  I'd say that
when people learn computer programming, they move up a gear, or two,
mentally.  And, using Glosa is a bit different from using natlangs, but not
as removed as computer programming.
   
>For now, I am certainly open to the possibiliy that Glosa
>may currently be structured in a way that allows for (or
>encourages?) phrases to be neither clearly noun-phrase, nor
>clearly verb-phrase. 
>
*   I'd say "essential" is the right word.  The secret of a language with
'syntax-based grammar' is that the ancient trick of "parsing and analysis"
has to work, and the clause and phrase structures need to be clearly
definable.
    However, Glosa has pretentions of being an auxiliary language, so, as
well as being universalised and standardised, it needs also to be
natural(istic) too.

>Perhaps they must always be more one than the other. Or
>perhaps there are ambiguities in the language that should
>be "fixed". Or perhaps there are desireable ambiguities.
>Or...?
>
*   While ambiguity is the lifeblood of language, and Glosa can do it as
                 well as any other language, it was proposed primarily a
standardised language for science; thus metphor and idiom should be avoided.

>I am not a linguist. I am enjoying learning about languages
>and how they affect (and are affected by) our thought
>patterns. I am mostly interested in finding, and using, a
>simple and effective IAL, whether it follows universal
>rules or breaks them.
>
*  And I will agree, Glosa is worthy subject of such study: much of the
problem is that Linguists are having trouble deciding what are the
'universals', or if there are any; and then, when they do come up with a
finding, it is often expressed in a language unintelligible to the common man.

Saluta,

Robin Gaskell

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] not all, but some letters.
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Fri, 9 Nov 2001 08:52:20 -0800 (PST)

--- Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au> wrote:
> At 18:59 03/11/2001 -0800, Kevin Smith wrote:
> >After writing fairly extensively in Glosa, I'm still
> > using ~du~. In fact, I'm using it more now than 
> > when I started.
> >
> *   My first reaction is recalling some Chinese learners
> of English, who
> were always saying things like,
>          "I going to the shop."
>     However, since your first language, kevin, is
> English, this reasoning can't be relevant.  I suspect
that 
> you have found a mental pathway which suits you, and 
> find it apt.
>     But the spirit of the language is the avoidance of
> unnecessary words,

My acid test is whether I can read what I've written. The
sentences I've written without ~du~ tend to be very
difficult for me to parse. In my mind, as I'm writing it,
it's obvious. But even two minutes later when I re-read it,
I often stumble.

~Mi~ means both 'I' and 'my', so that next word (e.g.
~hedo~ could be a noun, an adjective, an adverb, or a verb.
I don't want to have to read the whole sentence to figure
out what that second word is. 

If there are certain words which we choose to say CANNOT be
used as anything other than a verb, then I would omit ~du~
before them. Perhaps ~ki~ is an example. But that means we
cannot say: ~mi du ge-turba per tu ki~ [I am bothered by
your movement(s)]. 

This would set up exceptions in the language, which may be
helpful and necessary, but which would add complexity for
people learning it.

> and in the case of the SIMPLE PRESENT tense, the bare
> word being used as a
> verb is the clean form of the language.
>          ~Mi ki ad u boteka.~      SIMPLE PRESENT
>          ~Mi du ki ad u boteka.~   PRESENT CONTINUOUS

I agree there is a difference, so perhaps we need a true
verb marker. On the other hand, we accept that Glosa does
not distinguish between 'a' and 'the', so why should it
distinguish between simple present and present continuous?
(This question is serious, because I don't really know
enough about language issues to know the answer).

> *   This is good news - that you have done extensive
> wriiting.
>     Which fairy tale are working on rendering into Glosa?
>  I found it necessary to 'retell in Glosa' rather than to
> 'translate.'

The writing I have done is to translate my essays (mostly
related to software development), and to write notes to
this list. No fairy tales for me (yet).

I've done more translating to Glosa than writing in Glosa,
but have created at least a hundred original Glosa
sentences (probably quite a bit more). After I write in
Glosa, I usually translate that material to English as
well. 

In most cases I can roughly translate in either direction,
but there are definitely times when retelling is required.

Kevin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] not all, but some letters.
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Fri,  9 Nov 2001 11:20:13 -0800

Robin said:

>     But the spirit of the language is the avoidance of unnecessary words,
> and in the case of the SIMPLE PRESENT tense, the bare word being used as a
> verb is the clean form of the language.
>          ~Mi ki ad u boteka.~      SIMPLE PRESENT
>          ~Mi du ki ad u boteka.~   PRESENT CONTINUOUS

The first is not clear, because it can mean "my going to the store"
as well as "I go to the store". That's assuming "KI = to go".

I'd prefer to see KI as an auxiliary or light verb, and KINE as the
full concept word for "going" (and FU as redundant and ugly).

The use of pronouns like MI as possessive also is leading to
a lot of syntactic confusion.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] not all, but some letters.
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Fri,  9 Nov 2001 11:20:37 -0800

Robin says:

>     I believe that the French usage is the universalised form, and was
> convinced of this by Ron Clark.
>
>        In Glosa,   ~U hidra habe frigo.~  =HAS THE PROPERTY OF COLDNESS

Most languages AFAIK do not use "have", they use "is" for predications.

> if people translate their national
> idioms, metaphors, and natlang idiosyncratic syntax into Glosa, they are
> likely to write Glosa which is hard to read for people from other
> linguistic traditions.

That is what must always happen with any IAL.

>    2. New mindset suited to Glosa: yes!  When you learn a new natlang, you
> must try to get into the style of thinking of the native speakers of that
> language.  This is also true of an auxlang, but it is a bit harder,

The design of the IAL must allow for easy translation in both directions.
Simple translation is 95% relexing. The best IAL does not try to force
people to think in a different way, it aims for transparency.

A clear definition of 200 or so particles should be all that is
necessary to learn at the outset, then all the rest is just
filling in the vocabulary of concept words as needed.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] not all, but some letters.
From: "Paul O. Bartlett" <bartlett{at}smart{dot}net>
Date: Fri, 9 Nov 2001 20:02:28 -0500 (EST)

On Sat, 10 Nov 2001, Robin Gaskell wrote (small excerpt):

>     But the spirit of the language is the avoidance of unnecessary words,
> and in the case of the SIMPLE PRESENT tense, the bare word being used as a
> verb is the clean form of the language.
>          ~Mi ki ad u boteka.~      SIMPLE PRESENT
>          ~Mi du ki ad u boteka.~   PRESENT CONTINUOUS

    And the clean form of the language, in the avoidance of unnecessary
words, is to omit 'u' after 'ad' in these two instances, as 'ad' must
be followed by a noun structure.  Therefore, 'u' as a noun marker is
superfluous.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Ne and No (was: Updated web pages)
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 10 Nov 2001 07:54:19 -0800 (PST)

--- Sydpidd{at}aol{dot}com wrote:
> I'm using "ne" = opposite/negative/*-1 etc and 
> "no"= zero/not etc
> ne-boni=bad, no-kali=indifferent

This seems to be almost exactly opposite of the GID
meanings. 

GID:
ne     omit
no-    (opposite formed)

My own interpretation is that "ne-" is the lack of
something, and "no-" is the opposite, so:

ne-amo    don't like
no-amo    hate

ne-kali      not pretty
no-kali      ugly

Kevin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] du and u-mi
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 10 Nov 2001 08:26:11 -0800 (PST)

--- Sydpidd{at}aol{dot}com wrote:
> mi pa habe plu dubito ko "du" tem mi pa sti uti id sed id
> feno fo ko-uti. mi da sugesti "da" vice. 

Mi pluso pa pute; 'da' fu pote indika; proto akti-verba
frase. Alo, mi pluso amo alo 'ta', 'i', alo 'to'. Qo-ra,
hetero Glosa-pe du amo?

Exempla:
Mi sibi tako lekto
Mi sibi du tako lekto
Mi sibi da tako lekto
Mi sibi ta tako lekto
Mi sibi to tako lekto
Mi sibi i tako lekto

I also thought that ~da~ could indicate the start of a verb
phrase. Or I also like either ~ta~, ~to~, or ~i~. Which do
other Glosa speakers like?

Example:
My sibling(s) read(s) fast
My sibling(s) is a(are) fast reader(s)

> pluso il es plu de dubito per plu de pe de "u-mi"/"u mi",

> na posi uti "de mi" komo u frans-lingua. " u grama de mi"
 

Qe, tu du sugesti; na pan-tem uti 'de mi' pro ~my/mine~? Mi
ne volu koerce u-la forma. Deskribe-verba poli kron loka
pre u cef verba in Glosa.

Are you suggesting that we always use ~de mi~ to mean
'my/mine'? I don't want to enforce that form. Adjectives
and adverbs usually go before the main word in Glosa.

Kevin

========
To: glosalist <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] fatuo, stulti, stupidi
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 10 Nov 2001 10:31:26 -0800 (PST)

>From GID:

fatuo 1++        absurd; foolish; silly
stulti ++        fool; foolish
stupidi 1++      absurd; ridiculous; ruffian; stupid

I think ~no-intelige~ covers 'stupid'. 

I want to choose a single Glosa word to mean 'absurd,
foolish, silly, ridiculous', but I'm not sure which of
these would be best. I couldn't find any Greek roots for
them, and none seem to conflict with other Glosa words.

~fatuo~ has the ~uo~ that might be hard for some people to
pronounce? Perhaps it could evolve to ~fatu~, but that
sounds close to infatuation, which is almost an opposite
meaning.

~stulti~ is a bit hard for me to pronounce, but otherwise
seems fine. I can't think of a related English word, so I
couldn't look up the roots of this word. It seems to be a
spanish word, but I don't know if it came from Latin,
Greek, or elsewhere.

~stupidi~ is awfully close to the English 'stupid', so it's
probably not the best choice for 'foolish'.

I guess I'll go with ~stulti~, but would like feedback.

Also, 'ruffian' doesn't seem to belong on that list, since
it means 'bully', or someone who is mean or coerces others.
I'll probably just leave 'ruffian' off my list, and let
people build a word that conveys what they mean, such as:
~no-ami~, ~anti-pe~, ~koerce-pe~, etc.

Kevin

--- Sydpidd{at}aol{dot}com wrote:
> "ne" ab "negative"=-1 , "no" ab "none"=0
> Mi du puta ke ron pa dice homo u-co.

Posi, anti-co bi "18 gradu" e GID akorda; "no" = opositi, e
"ne" = ne/no habe. 

"18 gradu" du dice "ne = negative"; anti-co mi du kredi; id
pa semani u opositi a "affirmative", vice "-1". Plus, "18
gradu" du uti "ne" e "no" ne-uniformi--Marcel pa repara plu
kaso; anti-co mi du kredi; poli hetero kaso du habe ero.

U-ci pa kausa mi; tenta uti 'anti-' vice 'no-'. Mi nu-pa
sto, kausa mi pa logi; 'anti' du semani "oppose" e 'no-' du
semani 'opposite', qi ne homo. Mi sio amo trovi u alterna
verba vice 'no-'.

Kevin

Subject: [glosalist] ~sito~
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk>
Date: Sun, 11 Nov 2001 12:43:37 -0000

~Mi gene sko de Glosa.~
  I learn Glosa. 
     <- I get instruction about Glosa (from a teacher/book/internet etc)
     <- I am being given instruction about Glosa.
     <- I am being instructed about Glosa.

~Mi gene sito.~ 
  I eat.
     <- I get food.
     <- I am being given food.
     <- I am being feed.

Saluta,
Nick

Mikhail Adigeyev wrote
> >> I mean that for many (most?) words which are 'basically nouns', there
> >> is no 'normal' way of interpreting them as verbs.
> 
> NH> There are some general interpretations
> NH> 1) to use as a tool  ~akti per~
> NH>         hammer/to hammer         ~malea~
> NH>         telephone/to telephone    ~telefono~
> NH> 2) to use a means of transport  ~ki per~
> NH>         train/to go by train           ~trena~
> NH>         car/to drive (go by car)   ~vagona~
> NH> 2) to provide with  ~dona~
> NH>          frame/to frame (a picture)  ~qadra~
> NH>          colour/to paint                   ~kroma~
> I'd unite the former two into one class - 'to use'
> The last would include also cases like 'to turn into'.
> I see one more variant - 'to be/become'...

Any examples of 'to turn into' or 'to be/become' uses?

Another possible use 'to act like/in the capacity of' -
   (~akti homo / akti qa~)

~U paleo gina pa matri u ju-an, qi pa gene lose in urba.~

Saluta,
Nick

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ne and No (was: Updated web pages)
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sun, 11 Nov 2001 08:23:32 -0800 (PST)

--- Sydpidd{at}aol{dot}com wrote:
> "ze" ab "zero" for not, "anti" for opposite would I think
> be unambiguous
> ze boni/ze mali for indifferent
> anti boni for bad

I like ~ze~ instead of ~ne~, but ~ze~ is only in GID basic
as a suffix, so it would sort of be adding a word, which I
have so far avoided. I guess the other existing option
would be ~nuli~:
  ~nuli-boni/nuli-mali~ = indifferent
  ~no-boni~ = bad
But I like ~ze~ much better. But then, if someone asks you
a yes/no question, would you answer ~ja/ne~, ~ja/no~, or
~ja/ze~?

Unfortunately, I discovered that ~anti~ can't mean both
'oppose' and 'opposite', as they are quite different:

~anti-pe~ = opposing-person (opponent)
~anti-pe~ = opposite-of-person (?)

~anti-face~ = opposing-construction
~anti-face~ = opposite-of-construction (destruction)

> words using "-", how about compound phrases with "_"

Ugh. I don't like them in computer programming, and I would
really dislike them in a human language.

Kevin

if  we use "ze" we could have "ko-trika", "ze-ko-trika" (hairy, not 
hairy_smooth). there is no opposite
if we use "anti" for opposite, it might be unwise to use "ante" for anything.
we could use "no"/ne" for opposite, as there seems to be some confusion over 
"no" and "ne" they could both mean the same and perhaps one might fall by the 
wayside. they are shorter than "anti" which could seem a little clumsy by 
comparison.

<BR>if we use "anti" for opposite, it might be unwise to use "ante" for anything.
<BR>we could use "no"/ne" for opposite, as there seems to be some confusion over "no" and "ne" they could both mean the same and perhaps one might fall by the wayside. they are shorter than "anti" which could seem a little clumsy by comparison.</FONT></HTML>


--part1_151.3d9f90e.29202676_boundary

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] not all, but some letters.
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
From: "Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com" <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Sun, 11 Nov 2001 13:28:37 -0800

I wrote:

>> The first is not clear, because it can mean "my going to the store"
>> as well as "I go to the store".

Mikhail wrote:

> Theoretically it can.

And in practice.

> But I cannot create an example of a realistic
> sentence, which admit both interpretation.

That's because people don't actually talk or write in isolated
unrealistic sentences. I think the best/easiest thing is to translate
usenet postings in the colloquial way of writing/speaking.

> Perhaps, context always excludes all wrong interpretations?

I wish that were true but it isn't ... A real and useful
auxiliary language must be able to meet a higher standard,
Glosa cannot claim to have any special exemptions. Every
natural language has ways to specify with great precision.

So, I think that "good Glosa in practice" must use plenty
of those mono-syllabic particles for unambiguous syntax,
and use only multi-syllabic concept-words:

U gina pa kine ad urba (pro plu boteka).    (for the shops)
       ^^ ^^^^^^^^^^^^

Fe kine ad urba pa es sumptu.    Her trip to town was expensive.
   ^^^^^^^^^^^^ ^^

The general drift of all European languages has been toward
compound/periphrastic verb tenses, and Glosa followed that.
I believe it is the best way to go, for any IAL based on
"international scientific vocabulary". All case markings
are prepositioned, all compounding is head-last order.

In all these essential design elements, Glosa is similar to
my favorite IAL, Occidental. I'd like to see Glosa become
a more-highly-evolved version, with more carefully selected
particles and roots than at present. But there remains the
problem of converting all the familiar derivational suffixes.

Kevin Smith wrote
> --- Sydpidd{at}aol{dot}com wrote:
> > "ze" ab "zero" for not, "anti" for opposite would I think
> > be unambiguous
> > ze boni/ze mali for indifferent
> > anti boni for bad
> 
> I like ~ze~ instead of ~ne~, but ~ze~ is only in GID basic
> as a suffix, so it would sort of be adding a word, which I
> have so far avoided. I guess the other existing option
> would be ~nuli~:
>   ~nuli-boni/nuli-mali~ = indifferent
>   ~no-boni~ = bad
> But I like ~ze~ much better. But then, if someone asks you
> a yes/no question, would you answer ~ja/ne~, ~ja/no~, or
> ~ja/ze~?

~ne~ not, ~no~ no, and ~ze~ none.

There is an example in 18 Steps in the Richard II translation
where ~ze-pe~ is used for 'no man'.

There is a difference in the words. 
Compare ~ne-ge-game~  unmarried
    ~no-ge-game~ divorced
    ~ze-ge-game~ never-married

Saluta,
Nick

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ne and No (was: Updated web pages)
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sun, 11 Nov 2001 16:38:32 -0800 (PST)

--- Nick Hempshall <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk> wrote:
> ~ne~ not, ~no~ no, and ~ze~ none.
> 
> There is an example in 18 Steps in the Richard II
> translation where ~ze-pe~ is used for 'no man'.
> 
> There is a difference in the words. 
> Compare ~ne-ge-game~  unmarried
>     ~no-ge-game~ divorced
>     ~ze-ge-game~ never-married

But ~nuli~ is none, too. And ~no~/~ne~ seem to confuse just
about everyone at some point. Do we really need to
distinguish "not married now" from "not married and never
was"?

What if we pulled ~ze~ in from basic as a real word (not
just as a suffix), and generally used it instead of ~zero~.
Then, we also get rid of ~nuli~ because ~ze~ means the same
thing. Then, we avoid ~ne~ because it also means
(essentially) ~ze~. Finally, I'm tired of having ~no/non~
forms, so we don't have ~non~ either.

We would then have:
~no~ = opposite
~ze~ = zero, none, lacking,
~anti~ = oppose, against, etc.

~no-kali~ = ugly
~ze-kali~ = lacking beauty
~anti-kali~ = favoring ugliness, or preventing beauty

~no-memo~ = forget (used to know)
~ze-memo~ = no memory (never knew)

~ze-lo~ = nowhere, no place
~ze kron~ = never, no instance
~ze tem~ = instant, no timespan

~ze-fono~ = silent
~no-poli~ = few
~ze-dina~ = powerless, weak

I think I like it. It seems to be much easier to decide
between ~ze~ and ~no~ than it ever was to decide between
~ne~ and ~no~.

Kevin

u-ci es u frasi ex carlz (!)      
"I think Glosa needs better definiteness particles."       
an pa grafo id epi mo di de mo meno ..... a mo horo etc. an pa grafo durante 
u pusi longi de tem.      
na da habe o frasi e tem.         mi fu dona u nima frasi/tem zero.           
mi da tenta evita u jargono (mi da amo "jargono"!).        
de-nu, mi fu brevi u-na frasi a "I think something".   
       
nu, na da habe tri mero-frasi, u mero-frasi bi da habe plu koncepti "puta" 
pluso de tem (pa puta, fu puta), mero-frasi mo e tri da ze habe u tem 
koncepti.
       
u tem ko "puta", mi fu ge-nima "tem 1"   the time with "think"  I shall call 
"time1"        
mi puta ke frasi/tem0 es in pan lingua e multi lingua, si ze pan, da habe 
mero-frasi/tem1

here is a sentence from Charles :-
"I think Glosa needs better definiteness particles."
 he wrote it on one day, month ..... at mo hour etc. he wrote it during a 
small length of time.
  we have a sentence and time. I shall call it sentence/time zero
I shall shorten the sentence to "I think something".. 
 now, we have three part-sentences, the first has the concepts of "thinking " 
and also of time (thought, will think). the other two do not have this time 
concept.

Syd wrote:

> here is a sentence from Charles :-
> "I think Glosa needs better definiteness particles."
>  he wrote it on one day, month ..... at mo hour etc. he wrote it during a
> small length of time.
>   we have a sentence and time. I shall call it sentence/time zero
> I shall shorten the sentence to "I think something"..
>  now, we have three part-sentences, the first has the concepts of "thinking "
> and also of time (thought, will think). the other two do not have this time
> concept.

English often uses sentence patterns like this:
They are two separate problems for translation but
I just happened to combine them in the example.

1. at/in some time/place/condition, subject verb etc.
2. person thinks/believes (that) such-and-such.

The 2nd case should be simple enough in Glosa
if the elided THAT is restored; or one could try
to add "serial verb constructions" to the syntax.

The 1st case can be "fronted" similarly, since it is
a common enough tactic in many languages. I think.

tem1 du relatio a tem0.
in mero-frasi/tem1, "pa puta" pa acide pre pe pa grafo/dice/signi frasi/tem0, 
fu puta  fu acide  po u grafo.... e "da/du/i/ ... puta " da imbrika alo ki du 
ko u grafo. 
so, na du habe mero-frasi/tem1 pre-po-ko tem0
versa a frasi/t0, u mero-frasi mo "I/mi" pa existe pre m-frasi/t1 hetero 
"think/puta" sio ze acide so id es ms/pre t1, "something/uno-ra" ze posi 
existe a kron "think/puta" pa lase. so id es mf/po t1.
na nu da habe u tem seqe - mf/pre t1, mf/t1, mf/po t1



the time1 is related to time0. In part-sentence/time1, "thought" happened 
before  
sentence/time0 was written/spoken/signed, "will think" will happen after 
sentence/time0 and "think" overlaps, happens with s/time0, so we have 
part-sentence/time1-before, after or with time0 .

Going back to sentence/t0, the first part-sentence "I" existed before 
p-sentence/t1 otherwise "think" wouldn't have happened at all so it is 
part-sentence/before time1, "something" could not exist until "think" allowed 
it to, so it is part-sentence/after     time1. We now have a time order - 
ps/before t1, ps/t1, ps/after t1.

This, I think, is independant of the language used to express it.
Glosa uses this time order for its own word order. This too is independant of 
any other language
to translate   -  mero-frasi/pre tem1 (mf/pre t1) "mi"; mf/t1(ko t0) 
"da/du/i/ ..... puta"; mf/po t1 "uno-ra" 
mft1(pre t0)= "pa puta",   mft1(po t0)= "fu puta"



--part1_11a.6ff6220.2922de20_boundary

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Articles in Russian (was: not all, but ... )
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Thu, 15 Nov 2001 17:01:55 +1100

At 10:11 05/11/2001 +0300, Mikhail grafo:
>Charles pa grafo tem 2001-11-04:
>CCC> Mikhail pa grafo:
>>> Still, Russian does not use articles. And I couldn't say that >>>
Russian is less precise than English, in general.
>
>CCC> I believe that all natural languages can (and must) express
>CCC> all the same things. Over time, they all have converged to a
>CCC> similar level of expressiveness balanced with difficulty.
>CCC> (But an artificial language can be designed to be easier
>CCC> to learn by adults as a second language.) I'm sure Russian
>CCC> has ways to express definiteness without articles, just as
>CCC> English can express gender without inflections, and like
>CCC> Chinese expresses number without our style of plurals.
>Perhaps, you are right, and I just don't see those ways.
>Of course, we have construction like 'That very X, who ...'. But in
>other cases we normally don't use any words to express
>definiteness/indefiniteness.
>I would like to study this question. I suggest that you give me a
>number of sentences, in which definiteness is very significant. And I
>will tell you how they are translated in Russian...
>
*  This is a very meaningful challenge, and I will be interested to see how
things turn out.  Here is a slew of test sentences from me:-

  a. Id pa es u partikulari kapita-ve; qi un andra pa vide gene ge-vesti
ex u kausa-mort-pe.
   [it was the particular hat, which the man saw being worn by the murderer]

  b. Plu bi kanis pa viole plu tri felis.
    [the two dogs attacked the three cats]

  c. Bi kanis pa viole tri de plu felis.
    [two dogs attacked three of the cats]

  d. Bi de plu kanis pa viole plu tri felis.
    [two of the dogs attacked the three cats]

  e. U-ci pa eqa u tali di.
    [this  was   such a day]

  f. Plu agri pa habe plu vario kolori de grasi.
    [the fields had the various colours of grass]
     The fields were the various colours of grass.

  g. Fe loka plu ge-hidra vesti epi u grasi, habe kura de ne loka mu epi u
suela.
    [she puts the wet clothes on the grass, having care to not place them
on the soil]
     She puts the wet clothes on the grass, taking care not to place them
on the soil.

  h. Loka plu sperma intra ge-hidra suela.
    [place the seeds  in     wet    soil]
     Put the seeds into moist soil.

  i. An logi: homi nece casa bufalo.
    [he understands, mankind must chase buffalo]
     He understood, men need to hunt buffalo. 

  j. Tem seki grasi; an gene benifici exercisa. 
    [While cutting grass, he gets beneficial exercise]

>CCC> I guess that Russian would use "one" or "some" to indicate
>CCC> indefiniteness when necessary?
>We can say 'somebody', but it's third person. In 1st and 2nd persons
>gender specification is obligidary and cannot be skipped.
>
* k. An pa don id a uno-pe.
    [he gave it to someone]

  l. An pa don id ad u persona; qi es intra u kamera.
    [he gave   it to a person, who was in  the room]

  m. Uno-pe pa loka id la: mi demanda ke an, alo fe; qi pa akti so, sio
place loka id apo]
    [someone put it there, I ask that he, or she, who did so, (would)
please put it elsewhere]
     Someone put it there, would he, or she, who did so, please remove it.
     

  m'. Uno-pe pa loka id la: mi demanda ke u persona; qi pa akti so, sio
place loka id apo]
    [someone put it there, I ask that the person, who did so, (would)
please put it elsewhere]
     Someone put it there, would the one, who did so, please remove it.

  n. U ciani radikali ( -CN) es mo de plu toxi.
   [the cyanide radical is one of the poisons]

  o. U ciani radikali es u toxi; qi kausa tanto imedia morta.
   [the cyanide radical is a poison, which causes almost immediate death]

  p. U ciani radikali ne es u solo toxi.
   [the cyanide radical is not the only poison]

WITH ELISION
  p'. Ciani ne es u solo toxi.
     [cyanide is not the only poison]

>CCC> Many of the English uses for articles can be elided without
>problems.  My example illustrated the difference between "X is one of
>the Y" and "X is the only Y", which must be easy to express in any >language.
* q. Plu ju-an amo plu ju-fe.   | ... Plural markers, not determinants!
     [boys    like   girls]

  q'. Ju-an amo ju-fe.*         | ... Neither is good  
      [boy likes girl]*         |           usage!


>In Russian these two sentences would be normally translated the same
>way. Say, 'George Bush is a President' is just 'George Bush -
>President' (copula is omitted in usual speach). 'George Bush is the
>President of the United States' is 'George Bush - prezident
>Soyedinennyh Shtatov' (look at the absence of any determinator before
>'Soyedinennyh Shtatov' = 'the United States').
>
*  I suspect that in Glosa, the "descriptive" style should be used, at
first, while, with familiarity, elision will come in.

  r.  George Bush es u Presidenti de Plu Ge-unita Stato.

  s.  George Bush es u Presidenti.

  s'. George Bush es Presidenti.

  t   An es u Ge-unita Stato civi.
     [he is a United States citicen]

  t'  An es Unita-Stato civi.





>>> Sorry,  ..... I couldn't express my thoughts more clear :(((
>
>CCC> You write in English very well and clearly.
>Thanks. I had problems not only with my English, but with putting my
>thought in words, in general.
>
*  Agreed :)
   We all have trouble when wishing to use words to descrbe the way the
brain works!!!

Saluta,

Robin Gaskell

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Word Classes
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Thu, 15 Nov 2001 19:59:10 +1100

At 22:09 05/11/2001 -0000, Nick Hempshall wrote:
>I wrote
>> That raises the question as to what is a noun or a verb. 
>> 
>> It could be said that a noun is the name of something. But
>> is beauty a 'thing'? Isn't the adjective 'red' also the name
>> of a colour?
>> 
>> So definitions based on meaning have been replaced by
>> definitions based on structural features, that signal the 
>> way in which classes of words behave in a language.
>> 
>> Glosa's lack of inflexions make this determination of
>> the class of the head word less than obvious.
>
>Charles pointed out that inflexions have been replaced 
>by pre-positioned particles in Glosa. Whilst these are
>more likely to be omitted, than are inflexions, in texts, 
>they do give a route to a definition of word class based 
>on a word's relationship to them.
>
*  I do think that "Functional Grammar" is the more evolved thinking on the
way grammar works.

>For example one could start to define 'nouns' as any word that
>occurs as a head word in a phrase beginning ~u~ or ~plu~.
>
>~u mega tabla~ [table]
>~u bibli tri~       [book]
*  This usage is not clear.
   Is it book three of a series, or the third book when counting?
   I can't recall if there is a general affix to mean "number of a series",
as we have -th in English: it could be ~-ra~ [thing] in Glosa;
but I have a thought of something more like ~-me~ or ~-ime~.  But "TH" is
very close to "the" and "thing", so, ~ra~ could be it :-)
 
 Plu tri bibli           U tertia bibli    Numero-tri bibli
(the) three books        the third book    number three book

 U #3 bibli  OR  #tri bibli       U bibli-tri  .... ~U~ can be elided
 The No.3 book   No. three book    Book three

 The syntax for the NOUN PHRASE should be followed where it is logically
possible to do so:-

  NP indicator  - Numerator  -  Quantifier  - Qualifier(s) - 'noun'
 #determinant#
 OR 'pronoun'

    plu             tri           mega          bruno         ursus
       /mi    

>~plu poda de u zoa~   [leg] [animal] etc
>
>But then one might meet with
>
>~u vora~     [meal/eating]
>~u kanta~   [song/singing]  etc
>
>Are these words in the same word class?
*  It all depends on whether you are thinking in English or in Glosa!

  The nouned verb - or GERUND - is a very handy concept in English, and
while gerunds are functionally nouns in english, everyone knows they come
frome the verb class.

  English                        Glosa

 I like singing.               Mi amo kanta.

 I like to sing.               Mi amo kanta.

Please, please note:
  The Gerundive and the Infinitive in Glosa look and function the same:
they are interchangeable!
  It is only when you decide to translate the Glosa into another language,
OR think in that other language, that you need to place the Glosa
substantive, gerundive/infinitive into one class or another.

  However, for the simple 'nouning' of a Glosa substantive word, I would
say that the simple 'noun' meaning should be assumed - unless context
indicates otherwise.

IE  u vora  =  a meal; an eating    Possibly  u vora-ra ...
                                 to strss the tangible rather than 
                                 the more abstract gerundive concept.
    And then there are always the specific meals: eo-vora, meso-vora 
and vespera-vora.
    But there are always the gastronomes and the gluttons who like evry
meal: their situation may be covered, in Glosa, by saying ...

  ~Mu gluko singo vora-ra: mu simpli hedo vora.~ 
  - - - - - - - - - - - - - - - - - -
    u kanta  =  a song; a singing

  ~Mi fo hedo audi tu kanta; e mi ski tu fo amo kanta.~
[I very much like to hear your singing/song; and I know you very much love
singing/to sing]

  I can't really say the problem only arises when you try to convert the
Glosa into English, but I'd like to.
  Ron Clark used to say, '' IT IS ALL IN THE CONTEXT: the listener comes
halfway towards the speaker; we never have to treat language as stand-alone
.. there is always CONTEXT!''

  For the Glosa learner, the skill is in intuiting into the language:
perceiving the intent of the authors; and seeking out the various
'universal' constructions that were incorporated to make Glosa universal in
its functioning.  Above all, attempt to THINK IN GLOSA.

>Would one need to restrict the 'noun' class to words that
>*don't* occur as head words in phrases being ~du~?
>
>How about ~U ju-an du pa nasa u kuko.~?
>
>What class would ~nasa~ be? Is true to say it's a noun 
>being used as a verb? Or should be there be a separate 
>class of words that can do this? Are the following
>sentences correct?
>
>~U nasa de flori es gluko.~    [smelling a flower is nice]
>~U nasa de andro es rubi.~    [the man's nose is red]
>
*   On checking _Glosa 6000_, I discovered:-

       TO SMELL  nasa, olface

And, checking the other way:-

       OLFACE  smell

       NASA  nose

So, I'd say you can take your pick (no pun intended).

   However, my feeling is that a 'nasa' is more the thing on one's face,
while 'olface' is more the abstract quality that goes with detecting
smells.  So, for my own idiosyncratic view of language, saying " .. to nose
something .. " sounds more primitive than to " .. detect the olfactory
qualities of something .. ".

   Thus while, in Glosa, I definitely understand and enjoy reading that the
~ju-an pa nasa u kuko vora~, I would, myself, prefer to express it as ~u
ju-an pa olface u kuko vora.~

   But that skirts the question.  While there is a vast bunch of words that
might function as names, actions and descriptions, and sometimes all three
- called "substantives" in Glosa lore - it is patently obvious that some
words are much more related to things, others to actions, and some to
descriptions of things or actions.  So, although Ron Clark said any word
can funtion as any part of speech, the statement is not actually true, but
becomes so when you add two little words.
   Thus the mantra can be:-

          "In Glosa, any substantive word can function, WITHIN
            REASON, as any part of speech."

   So there, we get back to asking the communicator to think, and be
reasonable: maybe this is a tall ask. 
  - - - - - - - - - - - - - - 
   I would like to comment on Nick's examples, which look, on the face of
it, to be identical structures but with very different syntactic
undercurrents:-

>~U nasa de flori es gluko.~    [smelling a flower is nice] ... (1)  
>~U nasa de andro es rubi.~    [the man's nose is red]  ....... (2)

   And, sorry to say, I do feel it necessary to rearrange the words to
bring the underlying syntax to the surface.

(1)  Un olface de u flori dona gluko.
    [the smell of a flower gives sensory pleasure]

(2)  Un andro nasa habe rubi.
    [the man's nose has (the property of) red(ness)]

   Sad to say, I gave the overused "IS" a drubbing.  Actually, speakers of
English readily use the variant meanings of the word "is" without analysing
the situation.  But, "is" is basically a simple 'equals' sign.  And, if you
keep your English language usage of "is" to that, your use of language
becomes more colourful - and more accurate.

   If you learnt to speak French, you probably got a shock to find that
objects, in French, HAD or MADE qualities, not WERE qualities!

  English                  French             Glosa

The weather is cold.    Il fait froid.    U meteo habe frigo.

>PS I've now been reminded that ~plu~ occurs after 
>the possessive pronoun in standard Glosa texts, so it is not 
>always guaranteed to be the start of a noun-phrase-equivalent.
>Exempla - ~mi plu bibli~ [my books]
>
*   Agreed this does seem like an analogy.
  In the NOUN PHRASE, ~Mi plu bibli~ the pronoun takes precedence over the
plurality indicator.  Once the plurality of the books has been established,
the plurality marker can be elided, leaving the more semantically colourful
word, ~mi~, to do the dual work of starting the NP and establishing ownership.

Saluta,

Robin Gaskell

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ambiguous Sentences
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Thu, 15 Nov 2001 21:13:15 +1100

At 22:49 05/11/2001 -0000, Nick Hempshall grafo:

>Karo Glosa-pe,

>

>Tem lekto "mo-ok gradu", mi detekti u frase "Mu debi es in mi kamera". U-ci

>es ge-english per "They ought to be in my room", sed id posi semani u

>no-logi frase "Their duty is in my room". Qe u frase habe bi semani? Alo, qe

>id es mali Glosa?

>

*  E so, u-ci frasa veri habe bi semani, sed u sekunda semani habe mei

 logika - iso tu nota.  U verbi, ~debi~, usuali akti iso u 'verboid' te auxi u major verbi; qi funktio iso un akti-verbi.  U-ci uti pa es u tende de plu autori-pe in u-ci kaso: sed u regula ko maxi signifi es de detekti u kontextu.

   Tem plu frasa - intra reali bio - ne ne habe ge-isolato existe, e u kontextu debi sugere u korekti semani, u-ci regula ne dona libera pro no-kura kumunika.  Klu tena u frasa sine id kontextu, u semani de u frasa debi habe klari.  So, u-ci es un indika de boni frasa forma: singu frasa dona u pusi histori de id-auto.  Si id habe bi posi semani, id debi sio gene re-grafo.


>Kompara u frase "U soldata debi es milita pro an landa". Id habe tri

>ge-english frase "A soldier ought to be fighting for his country" e "The

>soldier's duty is to fight for his country"  e "A soldierly duty is to fight

>for his country". Proxi iso semani.

>

*  U mega forti de Glosa es ke plu-ci tri versio de u semani de u-ci frasa holo dona u homo histori.  U prima semani es u ge-tende semani.  ~Debi,~ itera habe u funktio de u 'verboid', pluso dona auxi ad u verbi, ~es.~

   Posi, per re-forma u frasa, na pote dona solo mo semani ad id:-


  ~U debi de u soldata es de milita pro an landa.~

  [the duty of a soldier is to fight for his country]


  ~U soldata debi milita pro an landa.~

  [a soldier ought to fight for his country.]


>Qe "They ought to be in my room" ge-dice in Glosa eqa "Mi ski nuli kausa; mu

>ne es in mi kamera" alo "Id sio es ma boni, si mu es in mi kamera"?

>

*  Bon eforti, Nick, tu grafo uti Glosa, e tu klari grafo.


Saluta, 


Robin Gaskell

========
To: Robin Gaskell <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re[2]: [glosalist] Articles in Russian (was: not all, but ... )
From: Mikhail Adigeyev <madigeyev{at}yandex{dot}ru>
Date: Thu, 15 Nov 2001 16:21:10 +0300

Robin pa grafo kron 2001-11-15:

RG> At 10:11 05/11/2001 +0300, Mikhail grafo:
>>Charles pa grafo tem 2001-11-04:
>>CCC> Mikhail pa grafo:
>>>> Still, Russian does not use articles. And I couldn't say that >>>
RG> Russian is less precise than English, in general.
I agree that Russian is less precise in things like modality, aspect
etc. But Russian adjectives, and, in some cases, verbs can express
subtleties which cannot be expressed in English.

RG> *  This is a very meaningful challenge, and I will be interested to see how
RG> things turn out.  Here is a slew of test sentences from me:-

RG>   a. Id pa es u partikulari kapita-ve; qi un andra pa vide gene ge-vesti
RG> ex u kausa-mort-pe.
RG>    [it was the particular hat, which the man saw being worn by the murderer]
"Eto byla ta samaya shliapa, kotoruyu, po svidetel'stvu muzhchiny,
nosil ubiytsa".
In this sentence "the particular ..." is translated like "ta samaya
..." ("that very ...")

RG>   b. Plu bi kanis pa viole plu tri felis.
RG>     [the two dogs attacked the three cats]
         Dve sobaki napali na tryoh kotov.
(just the same meaning as in English, except there are no articles).

RG>   c. Bi kanis pa viole tri de plu felis.
RG>     [two dogs attacked three of the cats]
If this mean that the dogs attacked three of a previously known group
of cats, in Russian it could be translated as
        Dve sobaki napali na tryoh iz nih.
        [Two dogs attacked three of them]

But if we don't have a special intention to be absolutely clear, we
would say it in the same manner as in (b). The listener can understand
which cats are meant, by context.

RG>   d. Bi de plu kanis pa viole plu tri felis.
RG>     [two of the dogs attacked the three cats]
Similar to (c). In usual speech it could be said as in (b).
But in speech 9not in writing) we could emphasize the intended meaning
my pronouncing "Dve" ("two") emphatically.

RG>   e. U-ci pa eqa u tali di.
RG>     [this  was   such a day]
        Eto byl takoy den'
A demonstrative is used here (similar to English).

RG>   f. Plu agri pa habe plu vario kolori de grasi.
RG>     [the fields had the various colours of grass]
RG>      The fields were the various colours of grass.
In Russian it would be expressed in a slightly different manner:
         Trava na plyah byla raznogo tsveta.
         [The grass on the fields was various colors]
No special particles are used here.
         
RG>   g. Fe loka plu ge-hidra vesti epi u grasi, habe kura de ne loka mu epi u
RG> suela.
RG>     [she puts the wet clothes on the grass, having care to not place them
RG> on the soil]
RG>      She puts the wet clothes on the grass, taking care not to place them
RG> on the soil.
         Ona kladyot mokruyu odezhdu na travu, starayas' ne polozhit'
         eyo na zemlyu.
No special particles are used.
BTW, what is the meaning of articles in this English sentence? Is it
different from
         She puts wet clothes on grass, taking care not to place them
         on soil
?
         
RG>   h. Loka plu sperma intra ge-hidra suela.
RG>     [place the seeds  in     wet    soil]
RG>      Put the seeds into moist soil.
         Polozhite zerna v     mokruyu zemlyu.
         [Put      seeds into  wet     soil]
No special particles are used.

RG>   i. An logi: homi nece casa bufalo.
RG>     [he understands, mankind must chase buffalo]
RG>      He understood, men need to hunt buffalo.
         On ponimayet, chto lyudi dolzhny ohotit'sya na bizonov.
         [He understands, that people (men) need to hunt buffalo]

RG>   j. Tem seki grasi; an gene benifici exercisa. 
RG>     [While cutting grass, he gets beneficial exercise]
         Podstrigaya travu, on priobretayet poleznyj opyt
No particles.

RG> * k. An pa don id a uno-pe.
RG>     [he gave it to someone]
         On dal eto komu-to
         [He gave this to someone]
If 'this' cannot be used here, than we should use either masculine
(he), or feminine (she), or neutral (it) pronoun, depending on the
gender of the object, referred by 'it'.
         
RG>   l. An pa don id ad u persona; qi es intra u kamera.
RG>     [he gave   it to a person, who was in  the room]
         On dal ego/eyo cheloveku, kotoryj byl v komnate
     lit.: [He gave "it/him/her" to man, which was in room]

RG>   m. Uno-pe pa loka id la: mi demanda ke an, alo fe; qi pa akti so, sio
RG> place loka id apo]
RG>     [someone put it there, I ask that he, or she, who did so, (would)
RG> please put it elsewhere]
RG>      Someone put it there, would he, or she, who did so, please remove it.
         Kto-to polozhil eto zdes'; Ya trebuyu, chtoby tot, kto sdelal
         eto, polozhil eto kuda-nibud' v drugoe mesto.
   lit.: [Somone put this here; I demand, that that[person], who did
         this, put this somewhere in another place]

Well, in Russian I'd put it in another manner, not so ugly as it
sounds in literal translation :)))

RG>   m'. Uno-pe pa loka id la: mi demanda ke u persona; qi pa akti so, sio
RG> place loka id apo]
RG>     [someone put it there, I ask that the person, who did so, (would)
RG> please put it elsewhere]
RG>      Someone put it there, would the one, who did so, please remove it.
The same as in (m).

RG>   n. U ciani radikali ( -CN) es mo de plu toxi.
RG>    [the cyanide radical is one of the poisons]
         Radikal tsianida - [eto] odin iz yadov
        [Radical of cyanide is one of poisons]

RG>   o. U ciani radikali es u toxi; qi kausa tanto imedia morta.
RG>    [the cyanide radical is a poison, which causes almost immediate death]
        Radikal tsianida - eto yad, kotoryj privodit k pochti
        mgnovennoj smerti.
        [Radical of cyanide is poison, which causes almost immediate
        death]

RG>   p. U ciani radikali ne es u solo toxi.
RG>    [the cyanide radical is not the only poison]
         Radikal tsianida - ne edinstvennyj yad.
        [Radical of cyanide (is) not only poison]
Russian has different words for an adverb 'only' and an ajective 'the
only'.

RG> WITH ELISION
RG>   p'. Ciani ne es u solo toxi.
RG>      [cyanide is not the only poison]
          Tsianid - ne edinstvennyj yad.

>>CCC> Many of the English uses for articles can be elided without
>>problems.  My example illustrated the difference between "X is one of
>>the Y" and "X is the only Y", which must be easy to express in any >language.
RG> * q. Plu ju-an amo plu ju-fe.   | ... Plural markers, not determinants!
RG>      [boys    like   girls]
         Mal'chiki ljubjat devochek

RG>   q'. Ju-an amo ju-fe.*         | ... Neither is good  
RG>       [boy likes girl]*         |           usage!
          Mal'chik ljubit devochku.

(q) can be understood as a universal statement, while (q') cant (it
says about one particular boy and one particular girl).
          
RG> *  I suspect that in Glosa, the "descriptive" style should be used, at
RG> first, while, with familiarity, elision will come in.

RG>   r.  George Bush es u Presidenti de Plu Ge-unita Stato.

RG>   s.  George Bush es u Presidenti.

RG>   s'. George Bush es Presidenti.

RG>   t   An es u Ge-unita Stato civi.
RG>      [he is a United States citicen]
          On - grazhdanin Soyedinyonnyh Shtatov.
          [he (is) citizen of United States).
          
RG>   t'  An es Unita-Stato civi.


RG> Saluta,

RG> Robin Gaskell


Saluta,
Mikhail
madigeyev{at}yandex{dot}ru

Robin Gaskell grafo (mero)
> >~u bibli tri~      

"The third book"

 *  This usage is not clear.
>    Is it book three of a series, or the third book when counting?

The meaning that goes with the context.

This is standard Glosa. See page 7 of "18 Steps", and page 12
of "Central Glosa".

5 books            penta bibli
the 5th book        u bibli penta

Also see "18 Steps" page 27

Change into 4th / 3rd gear        Kambio a tako tetra / tri.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Word Classes
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Thu, 15 Nov 2001 17:21:46 -0800 (PST)

--- Nick Hempshall <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk> wrote:
> Also see "18 Steps" page 27
> 
> Change into 4th / 3rd gear
> Kambio a tako tetra / tri.

I feel compelled to comment that "gear" seems to have been
literally translated to "tako" from the English bicyle
idiom "speed". Although it's probably still understandable,
it doesn't seem like the best translation.

I'm not sure we would really have to translate physical
gears grinding away, but it seems like there should be some
concept of discrete gear settings. Perhaps:

 ~Kambio a tako-gradu tetra~

Or on a bike, where the rider is very aware of the gear
wheels, maybe:

 ~Kambio a denti-ciklo tetra~

But then I start to question ~kambio~, which seems to be a
literal translation of "changing" gears, as if it were like
"changing" money (exchanging). Perhaps it would be better
as "shifting":

 ~kine a tako-gradu tetra~

Or, if this is a car and not a bike, perhaps even:

 ~kine u dina-me a tako-gradu tetra~

Or, if we accept that you are really "changing" something:

 ~muta u tako-gradu a tetra~

Kevin (nit-picker) Smith

To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>

From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk>
Date: Fri, 16 Nov 2001 22:18:07 -0000

> > Also see "18 Steps" page 27
> > Change into 4th / 3rd gear
> > Kambio a tako tetra / tri.
> 
Kevin Smith wrote
> I feel compelled to comment that "gear" seems to have been
> literally translated to "tako" from the English bicyle
> idiom "speed". Although it's probably still understandable,
> it doesn't seem like the best translation.
> But then I start to question ~kambio~, which seems to be a
> literal translation of "changing" gears, as if it were like
> "changing" money (exchanging). 

Yes. I agree with you that both ~tako~ and ~kambio~
seem to be the wrong choice here.

> Perhaps it would be better
> as "shifting":
> 
>  ~kine a tako-gradu tetra~
> 
> Or, if this is a car and not a bike, perhaps even:
> 
>  ~kine u dina-me a tako-gradu tetra~

Like it.

> Or, if we accept that you are really "changing" something:
> 
>  ~muta u tako-gradu a tetra~

I thought about ~muta~ as well. But this would be transforming
something into something else, so that the original thing is now
not available to change back to and has to be re-created. At least
with 'exchange' you are just swapping between use and non-use.
Even if it sounds like you might need to call in a car mechanic to do it!

How about ~Elekti u ratio tetra~ [Choosing the fourth ratio.]?

Saluta,
Nick

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Word Classes
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Fri, 16 Nov 2001 17:45:46 -0800 (PST)

--- Nick Hempshall <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk> wrote:
> > > Also see "18 Steps" page 27
> > > Change into 4th / 3rd gear
> > > Kambio a tako tetra / tri.

> > as "shifting":

> >  ~kine u dina-me a tako-gradu tetra~
> 
> Like it.
> How about ~Elekti u ratio tetra~ [Choosing the fourth
> ratio.]?

Mi i fo-amo u 'elekti'. Mi i ze-amo u 'ratio', kausa id i
ze-ge-taxo a'kordi'. Posi, id fu debi ge-taxo u 'kordi'.
Alo, mi i amo:

  elekti u tako-gradu tetra

Kevin

To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] All-clear signal: www.glosa.org is clean !
From: "Marcel Springer" <m.springer{at}nikocity{dot}de>
Date: Sat, 17 Nov 2001 18:49:32 +0100

Ne-risko-semani:  http://www.glosa.org es puri e ne habe virus!  Place
sti visita plu pagina itera; kron vi volu.  Saluta ex Marcel.



[And here is the story, which has otherwise nothing to do with Glosa:

Mikhail Adigeyev grafo, 2001-11-09:
 > Since I've just edited english variant of the page that I've
 > downloaded from www.glosa.org, my guess is that the server might be
 > infected. Marcel cured the file I've sent him, so
 > http://www.glosa.org/brevi/ruski.htm is free from viruses now.
 >
 > But Marcel has no time to check other pages in the site. So he asked
 > me to warn all of you that there is a possibility that Glosa site i
 > infected. He can check the site in 7 days only, not earlier.

When I received Mikhail's translation of the Glosa short information
( http://www.glosa.org/brevi/ruski.htm , thank you, Mikhail!), it was
infected with a virus.

I checked my computer and it was clean.  Mikhail checked his one, and
it was clean, too.  So, the virus could have been on www.glosa.org ,
where he has downloaded the text from, or on the way of e-mail
transfer.

When Mikhail wrote to me, his computer were clean, I knew, I would
have to warn any possible visitor of the site and then exclude the
possibility of an infected www.glosa.org .  But just when I got his
reply, I was in a hurry.  I was just about to leave home for eight
days and for that I had unsubscribed Glosalist before.  So I myself
could not warn you and asked him, if he would do me the favour to do
it.

Now I am back and I have downloaded all files from www.glosa.org and
have checked them with the latest version of the antivirus software
F-Prot ( ftp.complex.is/pub/fp-311.zip ) -- and no virus was detected.

Then I have had a look at all 65 *.htm-files, searching for any
scripts or suspicious attachments -- and I found nothing wrong.

After this I am convinced, that there is no virus on www.glosa.org !

Although Mikhail and me were lucky having no data damaged, this
testing was an annoying loss of time for us and I am very sad about
this world full of destructiveness.  Remember the rebounce for Glosa,
when Robin's PC got a virus and a lot of valuable Glosa material was
lost.]

na dua nece u mega,mega lexiko e na pote detekti plu multi verba ex 
engelingua etc na posi uti e grafo in glosa. si u-co lexiko dua klude multi 
multi verba, plu de mu fu es plu simile. tem na skope mu in na lexiko, na 
posi elekte. na sio ge-auxi si singu verba pluso habe u numera tem id pa uti 
e u signi te  monstra u id "nit-splitting-hair-picking index", plu doxo de 
plu an lexi-grafo-pe - fo ple de auxi.    
tem na dua elekte mu, na sio puta de:-
mu fu es ma boni si singu verba, si pote, dua sti ko konsona e sto ko vokali.
pluso singu verba posi fu es CVCVCVCV etc. id sio ma boni te eludu  VV, VVV, 
CC, CCC etc
posi na pote eludu "x" e "q" , "x" ="ks" (CC)
plu de pe idio de sud asia du habe u dificile si b, c, d, g, k, p e t du ne 
ge-seqe per u vokali
xanto/leuko    na du habe  x, ks (CC) (kC) e nt (CC)
leuko  du habe eu (VV) sed ze (VV)  
leuko ge-uti in engelingua "leukaemia" sed plu enge-pe du dice " l u k i m i 
a", glosa pote uti "luko"

http://hometown.aol.co.uk/sydpidd/myhomepage/business.html 

we need a large large dictionary so that we ( where's this size font come 
from?)  choose the many words from our native tongues to express 
ourselves.there would be similes but we could gradually choose the most 
popular ones and use the others less and less. if each word were marked by 
its frequency of use and by a spit-nitting- pair-hicking index ( our 
lexicographers have some very useful ones) it would help our choice.
we could also bear in mind it would be easier to follow words one with 
another if they all started with a consonant and finished with a vowel, glosa 
was designed to have CVCVCVCV...... alternating for ease of pronunciation. we 
could try to avoid consonant and vowel clusters. there are dissenters from 
our use of x and q.
some nationalities have difficulty with the following if they are not 
followed by a vowel
b c d g k p t (x=ks)
xanto  we ks and nt  (CC CC) , kC
leuko  we have only eu (VV)  kV is ok
in english we say l oo k ee m i a for lekaemia  so glosa could have luko


sid

We need a huge word list when translating from Glosa. We
should use a small word list when translating into Glosa.
My word list tries to serve for translating into Glosa. The
GID serves to help oneunderstand written Glosa material.

I have already worked hard on my list. Perhaps it could be
our initial small list, that we would modify?

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Word Classes
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Sat, 17 Nov 2001 14:27:34 -0800

sid wrote:

> we need a large large dictionary so that we

I think a standard Latin dictionary is the best choice.

> ( where's this size font come from?)

Probably you used a HTML editor instead of a plain text editor
to compose your message. This is still having the bad result
of sending both HTML and plain text to this email list ...

> choose the many words from our native tongues to express
> ourselves.there would be similes but we could gradually choose the most
> popular ones and use the others less and less. if each word were marked by
> its frequency of use and by a spit-nitting- pair-hicking index ( our
> lexicographers have some very useful ones) it would help our choice.

I agree, at this point. I would select Latin verbs in the infinitive,
and derive the Glosa form, something like this: (and it's tempting
to use "-re" to mark the finite verb or true infinitive in Glosa)

docere --> doce = teach
dolere --> dole = suffering
ducere --> duce = to lead
durare --> dura = hard, lasting

> we could also bear in mind it would be easier to follow words one with
> another if they all started with a consonant and finished with a vowel, glosa
> was designed to have CVCVCVCV...... alternating for ease of pronunciation. we
> could try to avoid consonant and vowel clusters. there are dissenters from
> our use of x and q.
> some nationalities have difficulty with the following if they are not
> followed by a vowel
> b c d g k p t (x=ks)
> xanto  we ks and nt  (CC CC) , kC
> leuko  we have only eu (VV)  kV is ok
> in english we say l oo k ee m i a for lekaemia  so glosa could have luko

It is possible to eliminate all of KQWXYZ in spelling the Latin roots.
But there are still many FL FR GR GL CT NG SP ST etc combinations.
Simply allow for a pronounced (but unwritten) schwa to break them up.
Spelling the Latin roots *correctly* in Glosa adds educational value,
and makes vocabulary learning much easier in both directions.
But the Greek roots are impossible and must be eliminated.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Word Classes
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 17 Nov 2001 14:35:53 -0800 (PST)

--- Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com wrote:
> It is possible to eliminate all of KQWXYZ in spelling the
> Latin roots.
> But there are still many FL FR GR GL CT NG SP ST etc
> combinations.
> Simply allow for a pronounced (but unwritten) schwa to
> break them up.

I agree. For example, Ptero could be pronounced roughly
'puh-tero'. Xeno can be spoken as 'zeno' or roughly
'kuh-seno'.

I still have mixed feelings about eliminating q and x.

> But the Greek roots are impossible and must be
> eliminated.

I completely disagree. There are already plenty of
Romance/Latin clone IAL's out there. People who want a
Latin-based language can choose one of them. Glosa's use of
Greek makes it slightly more fair to non-Europeans.

Kevin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Ambiguous Sentences
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 17 Nov 2001 19:39:03 -0800 (PST)

--- Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au> wrote:
> >Kompara u frase "U soldata debi es milita pro an landa".
> *  U mega forti de Glosa es ke plu-ci tri versio de u
> semani de u-ci frasa holo dona u homo histori.  U prima
> semani es u ge-tende semani.  ~Debi,~ itera habe u
> funktio de u 'verboid', pluso dona auxi ad u verbi, ~es.~
> 
>    Posi, per re-forma u frasa, na pote dona solo mo
> semani ad id:-
>   ~U debi de u soldata es de milita pro an landa.~
>   [the duty of a soldier is to fight for his country]

Boni. Qo-tende tu pa uti ~de~? Qo-tende no:
 U debi de u soldata es milita pro an landa

Sed, milita-pe du pote es an alo fe. Veri, mi ma-amo:
  U debi de u milita-pe es milita pro id landa
alo ~pe landa~? alo ~se landa~? 

>   ~U soldata debi milita pro an landa.~
>   [a soldier ought to fight for his country.]

Mi ma-amo:
  U milita-pe du debi milita pro [an/fe/pe/se/id] landa

Kausa id du pote semani: [a soldier's duty fights for his
country]

Kevin

plu intro-verba, preloka-verba, particles, prepositions etc
te sti u tem-mero-frasi/aktivi-verba-grupo, na posi habe - 
ze verba, u-co es homo paleo glosa     id es dificili de logi per plu de pe, 
mu es multi exempla
"du"  plu de na du puta ke id du face u frasi ma facile de logi, du es nu in 
glosa, id du semani uno-ra pusi hetera in paleo g, mi du amo id posi kausa id 
es homo "do" in engelingua in soni e funktio, id es CV, id es facile de dice, 
id es 145 in utiglosa 
"da " id es CV, id es neo a glosa, id ze es homo uno-ra hetera, id es facile 
de dice, il es 19, id da memo plu an ruski ami de "da"=ja
kausa alo glosa verba fu memo uno-pe, uno-lo de u verba in u-pe auto lingua, 
mi fu ze numera id
"dua" id es CVV, id es pusi longi, il es 0,  id es neo a glosa
ta id habe u difere semani nu in g, id es CV, id es facile te dice
"i"  id es V - normali id i seqe u finale vokali  VV sed id es ze dificili de 
dice, id es neo 
a-nu, mi sio posi dona u ge-divina indexi homo seqe
ze intro-v......... 8 
du................... 10
da...................  8
dua.................  6
i .....................  7
tem singu pe du grafo u-co verba, mu fu ge-numera e u pikti posi fu muta

sid

--part1_107.8d37403.29295c47_boundary

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] picnicking-pearslicking-index
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sun, 18 Nov 2001 11:33:51 -0800 (PST)

I don't know what pearslicking is, but...

--- Sydpidd{at}aol{dot}com wrote:
> a-nu, mi sio posi dona u ge-divina indexi homo seqe
> ze intro-v......... 8 
> du................... 10
> da...................  8
> dua.................  6
> i .....................  7
> tem singu pe du grafo u-co verba, mu fu ge-numera e u
> pikti posi fu muta

Mi pa puta: Posi 'du' sio semani "English: do", e 'nu-du'
sio semani "English: now doing". U-ci du hetero de paleo
Glosa, sed ze fo-hetero.

Mi du kanti. (I sing)
Mi nu-du kanti. (I am now singing)

Alo, mi sio taxo (10 = fo-boni, 1 = fo-mali):
> ze intro-v......... 3 (freqe ze-klari)
> du................... 5 (konfusi ko paleo)
> da...................  9
> dua.................  8
> i .....................  7

du vice nu-du....... 8

Kevin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Word Classes
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Wed, 21 Nov 2001 03:19:49 +1100

At 22:53 05/11/2001 -0000, you wrote:
>> Are these words in the same word class?
>> Would one need to restrict the 'noun' class to words that
>> *don't* occur as head words in phrases being ~du~?
>> 
>                                                         beginning ~du~
>
*  Friends, World Citizens, Communicators,

  Despite "terrorism", the world is actually moving into a new phase.

  The thinking brains on this Planet are loostening up: is it something in
the water, an increase in cosmic waves, or just plain old evolution of
civilisation?

  The static thinking, of rigidified categories in a classification imposed
upon us by 'academics,' is being left behind.  People are starting to think
in abstract concepts and symbols: the old "a noun is a noun is a noun"
mentality is giving way to the 'idea of thingness.'

  Yes, we still have 'hard-wired syntactic structures' in our brains, but
people are ^pushing the envelope^, attempting to find novel ways of saying
things - and thinking about things and actions.  Perhaps, just perhaps, a
new and fluid medium for the expression of ideas is called for .. and
emerging .. despite the traditional restrictions of the  traditional,
'concrete' mediums of communication.

  We still don't uderstand the metaphor process, and can't accurately tell
when we are reaching the boundary between metaphors and real symbols for
things and actions --- but that still does not stop us from trying to
extend our use of metaphor and other colourful language (figures of speech).

  And maybe, just maybe, a new more flexible non-rigid communication medium
will emerge in the world .. possibly as a universal language type of thing
.. and possibly something like Glosa!!!

  Was Ron Clark right .. in attempting NOT to confuse people with a
prescriptive Glosa grammar-book?  Was he hoping that people had the
intellectual capacity to use the symbol for the concept they wished to
convey - neither as this part of speech, nor that - but as a 'unit of
thought' placed into a meaningful communication, and made sense of
according to its syntactical relationships?  Or was he running too fast, as
he rushed ahead bearing his 'communicator's torch' - leaving the rest of us
behind with our traditional views of language.

  And so, in less flowery language, Ron was attempting to say that a word
was a symbol for an idea, not a "noun" or a "verb".  Our brain tells us
that some symbols are predominantly understood to be 'thingy' while others
are 'active' - or even almost exclusively one or the other-
but, then, along comes the poet, the novelist ... the writer of modern
lyrics or the person who creates the names for American movies, and we
discover that the old categories and their boundaries can be breached - and
often meaningfully, and creatively, so.
  I suspect that this was the sort of thinking that led Ron Clark to say,
"A word can be used, within reason, as any part of speech."

  Perhaps I could write a fantasy in which, a small green bricks a fast, or
something like that - in Glosa, of course.  But this might be trying too
hard to test the boundaries of word categories.  But, back in pedestrian
language, the correct use of syntax, together with the avoidance of
non-literal language, should ensure that all Glosa sentences are clearly
understandable.
  
  ~Sed, te itera veni a norma lingua, u korekti uti de sintaxi, plus un
evita de no-exakti lingua, sio debi dona asura ke holo plu Glosa frasa habe
klari semani.~

Saluta,

Robin Gaskell

========
To: glosalist <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] Re: 18 steps copyright?
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Tue, 20 Nov 2001 08:21:27 -0800 (PST)

I wrote to ask Marcel if I could get permission to update
the "18 steps" document to conform to my unofficial core
list (www.qualitycode.com/glosa).

His response politely suggested that perhaps I should not
tamper with this classic work of art (which other folks are
also working on at the moment), and that my time might be
better spent updating some of the other material.
[Hopefully that's a reasonable summary. Marcel?]

I composed this response to send to him directly, but then
realized that it is quite relevant to the whole group. So
here it is:

---

In my mind, one of the very large obstacles to adoption of
Glosa is that there are now FIVE distinct dialects out
there:
1. 18 steps/PGN
2. Glosa Basic Reference
3. GID core/basic
4. Syd Pidd's reforms (trailing vowels)
5. My own word list

Someone who uses words from any of the five will have some
difficulty communicating with any of the others. I hope
that material created based on my list would be the most
widely understandable because generally my list is a subset
of the others.

My big concern is that "18 Steps" is being listed as the
MOST official source, and it's also the only lengthy
tutorial available. The web page says it is "the best way
to learn Glosa." It is also the most widely translated
material. This will lead new people to learn from it
first...at which point they will find many, many words
which are not in any of the other references.

If "18 Steps" is really an unchangable work of art, it
should be described as that. People should NOT use it as a
first step towards learning Glosa. They should only look
back at it after they have learned "modern" Glosa to gain
perspective.

If we actually want people to learn from "18 Steps", it
must be updated, at least to GID Basic (Core would be
better), if not to my own list. It is disturbing to realize
that Russians will be learning what some of us consider to
be an obsolete dialect of Glosa.

My other option is to create my own tutorial, from scratch,
using my word list. Obviously this would be much more
difficult.

So I close with these questions, to which I would really
like to hear your own personal answers:

What are your goals for the Glosa langauge and community? 

What are the one or two projects someone could do right now
that would have the most impact towards those goals?

What do you see as the biggest weaknesses of the language
(and/or its community) right now? 

Kevin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] 18 steps copyright?
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Tue, 20 Nov 2001 11:29:09 -0800

Kevin wrote:

> I wrote to ask Marcel if I could get permission to update
> the "18 steps" document to conform to my unofficial core
> list (www.qualitycode.com/glosa).

I don't think anyone can/should "update" or otherwise edit
any copyrighted work without the permission of the author.
Marcel does have a working relationship with the author and
clearly tries to respect her wishes. We others are free to
produce our own works, of course.

But how can we write with any authority about Glosa unless
we have first obtained substantial agreement with many other
Glosa users and/or authorities? I've been on this list for
several years and witnessed very little (or no) agreement
about changing or extending Glosa. And I'd only consider
Wendy or her anointed successor as the true authority.
(I speak personally as just one among the heretics.)

> In my mind, one of the very large obstacles to adoption of
> Glosa is that there are now FIVE distinct dialects out

Not really. Only publications of Ron and Wendy are official,
the rest is "merely" amateur fan-club stuff.

> that material created based on my list would be the most
> widely understandable because generally my list is a subset
> of the others.

It is merely your personal list. On the Occidental email lists,
learners are encouraged to create their own mini-dictionaries.
This is always very useful, but non-canonical. No language has
a closed vocabulary, unless it is dead (unused in real life).
I see the G vocabs as exemplary but provisional; the learner
is supposed to catch onto the patterns of word-formation.

> My big concern is that "18 Steps" is being listed as the
> MOST official source

Marcel is publishing many pages about Glosa on the web,
and surely more people will learn from there than from
any other source. Glosa isn't like a C compiler, it isn't
an exact science and never can be. Lojban has a sort of
automatic translator, though, and you might prefer it.
(I've also mentioned my own online tranlation page.)
But my understanding is that real language just doesn't
work like that (i.e., it is very unlike Lojban or Java).

> If we actually want people to learn from "18 Steps", it
> must be updated

I'd toss all the R&W stuff onto the obsolete v2 pile
and migrate to version 3, if there ever will be such.
I have found several other IALs to be far more usable
than Glosa in its current state, and by a large margin.

In a message dated 11/20/01 04:22:34 GMT Standard Time, kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com 
writes:


> 4. Syd Pidd's reforms (trailing vowels)

i look upon those reforms more as drawing attention  to/revealing  what is 
already there in glosa but is not always obvious. the trailing vowels are not 
enormously important and the experiment with "nng" is something I shall look 
at later, perhaps an item of curiosity. however the other odds and ends could 
be more useful.
my uti-lexiko, I have extended a little as the hyphens cause a problem or 
two. I have updated the web page to include items in glosalist of the first 
half of november and added a list of the words etc minus hyphens and another 
of hyphenated phrases. incidentally, when I write glosa, I use central glosa 
most and burrow into the collection of 6000 and core etc for a few words i 
need but are not in CC
syntax.....
it seems to me that g has something like the following
sentences/plu frasi
part sentences/ plu mero-frasi
these part sentences are ordered by time; the production and delivery of 
sentences  start and finish at definite times, the space between, I shall 
call "time 0"
one part sentence expresses time "think", "am thinking", thought", "will 
think". I shall call this "time 1". Time1 is related to time0. "thought/pa 
puta" started and finished before time0, "will think/fu puta" will start and 
finish after time0, "think" etc overlap, straddle or are with time0. to make 
it clear, i am using "du puta" here.
in "u gina du/pa/fu face uno-ra" "u gina" cannot express time "pa 
gina/womanned(!)", "fu gina/will/woman(!)" but she existed before/with/after 
time1 (she couldn't have made anything, otherwise). the something happened 
after the start of time1
in time order we have:-
part sentence before time1/mero-frasi-pre-tem1 - part sentence time1(related 
to time0)/mero-frasi-tem1 - part sentence after time1
or  (in jargono)      subject phrase- verb phrase - object phrase 
this is glosa as was, but in terms of time and avoiding technical terms. I 
have taken the liberty to purloin an existing word in glosa (du) to act as 
particle for time1 and time0 co-inciding 
The technical term "particle", I am calling introduction word/intro-vera
syd
