To: Robin Gaskell <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re[2]: [glosalist] Word Classes
From: Mikhail Adigeyev <madigeyev{at}yandex{dot}ru>
Date: Thu, 22 Nov 2001 19:50:43 +0300

Robin pa grafo kron 2001-11-20:

...
RG>   The static thinking, of rigidified categories in a classification imposed
RG> upon us by 'academics,' is being left behind.  People are starting to think
RG> in abstract concepts and symbols: the old "a noun is a noun is a noun"
RG> mentality is giving way to the 'idea of thingness.'
...
RG>   Was Ron Clark right .. in attempting NOT to confuse people with a
RG> prescriptive Glosa grammar-book?  Was he hoping that people had the
RG> intellectual capacity to use the symbol for the concept they wished to
RG> convey - neither as this part of speech, nor that - but as a 'unit of
RG> thought' placed into a meaningful communication, and made sense of
RG> according to its syntactical relationships?  Or was he running too fast, as
RG> he rushed ahead bearing his 'communicator's torch' - leaving the rest of us
RG> behind with our traditional views of language.

RG>   And so, in less flowery language, Ron was attempting to say that a word
RG> was a symbol for an idea, not a "noun" or a "verb".  Our brain tells us
RG> that some symbols are predominantly understood to be 'thingy' while others
RG> are 'active' - or even almost exclusively one or the other-
RG> but, then, along comes the poet, the novelist ... the writer of modern
RG> lyrics or the person who creates the names for American movies, and we
RG> discover that the old categories and their boundaries can be breached - and
RG> often meaningfully, and creatively, so.
RG>   I suspect that this was the sort of thinking that led Ron Clark to say,
RG> "A word can be used, within reason, as any part of speech."

Well, I've been pondering about the problem few years ago, mostly in
connection with my interest in problems of AI and world
rpresentations... And I had come to a conclusion, that difference
between nouns and verbs is inherent to our human way of thinking. They
are basic categories, and we just cannot think other way. It's similar
to our 3-dimensional model of the world - we just cannot think in
terms of 4-dimensional space or 2-dimensional time...
More precisely, I've come to the following model. All 'objects of
thoughts' can be classified according to their ability to be located
in space and time.
Thus, we have 4 classes:
1) Time intervals: have hard location in time and no location in
space. E.G.: Year 2001.
Time intervals can be identified just be their position in calendar.
2) Spots: have hard location in space and no location in time. E.G.:
Russia.
To identify a unique spot, we should specify its location, relative to
other spots.
3) Situations: have hard location both in space and in time.
We can specify situations by locating them in space and in time (and
specifying the sort of situaion, eg: running, eating, etc).
4) Things: have no location in either space or time. Things are
identified by their unique sets of attributes. When we meet a person
twice in different locations and in different moments, we still
understand that he is the same person :)

To say roughly, verbs describe sorts of situations and nouns describe
sorts of things. This is always true. Say, noun 'rain' describes
a situation (it should be specified both in time and in space -
today's rain is not the same 'object' as yesterday's, and rain in
Washington is not that very 'object', as rain in Moskow, even if they
are simultaneous).

So, I believe that we _do_ think in terms of things and situations,
and any language should have this distiction in it.


RG> Saluta,

RG> Robin Gaskell

Saluta,
Mikhail
madigeyev{at}yandex{dot}ru

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Word Classes
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Thu, 22 Nov 2001 10:44:26 -0800

Robin pa grafo kron 2001-11-20:

>> Was Ron Clark right .. in attempting NOT to confuse people with a
>> prescriptive Glosa grammar-book?

Yes, because that was his intention and Hogben's, to escape
from Latin/Greek grammarians and the failed foreign language
teaching approaches of the postwar period. And yes, because
the pidgin-creole approach is probably the best/easiest/fastest
way to connect people of vastly different cultural backgrounds.
Examples: Lingua Franca (Sabir) and the Chinook Jargon.

Mikhail wrote:

> And I had come to a conclusion, that difference
> between nouns and verbs is inherent to our human way of thinking.r

> All 'objects of
> thoughts' can be classified according to their ability to be located
> in space and time.

> So, I believe that we _do_ think in terms of things and situations,
> and any language should have this distiction in it.

Linguists say there are more like 50 or 100 "parts of speech".
They vary a bit in each language but with a lot of similarity.
Rather than give a philosophical name and description to each
official category, though, we could define them operationally,
by means of "sentence frames". This follows Ron's intuition.
I think there should be approx 50 or 100 of them ...

the FIFTH cat ... ordinal number
the FIVE cats ... cardinal number

that SEX-ACT was legal ... deverbal noun
he SEX-ACTED her ... transitive verb
she got SEX-ACTED ... intransitive-passive

the GREEN was ugly ... deadjectival noun
the GREEN colors ... plain adjective

I'm not good at collecting samples, sorry.  Anyway, what's
important is the left-hand side of the examples, and their
Glosa translations, not the right-hand side terminology.
A better set of frames came with WordNet, but I can't
find them now.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] reforms
From: Sydpidd{at}aol{dot}com
Date: Thu, 22 Nov 2001 15:26:58 EST

--part1_135.509ffc5.292eb992_boundary
Content-Type: text/plain; charset="US-ASCII"
Content-Transfer-Encoding: 7bit

intro-vera should be intro-verba
basically, a g mero-frasi/part sentence would have:
intro-verba, word helping the next word, word helping the next word, head 
word, preposition phrases helping the head word. 
the next intro-verba or pre-loka-verba would point to the head word before it 
-
"the thin and very tall man with long and dark brown hair is ...... "
"the" introduces, "very" helps "tall", "thin" and "tall" help "man", "with" 
shows that "man" is the head word, "dark" helps "brown", "long" and "brown" 
help "hair", "is" begins the next part sentence.
"u tenu e fo alti andro ko longi e skoto bruno kapila es ......    "
plu de intro-verba:-
"pa" introduces a part sentence with time/mero-frasi-tem, also states 
relation between the time and the time of another part sentence 
"fu" does as "pa" does
"du" does as "pa" does but is less essential - as "es" is used almost always 
as mero-frasi-tem, it can be left out as the speaker wishes
"u" intros a mero-frasi-pre/po-tem, also carries single number
if pre-tem, it comes before the mero-frasi-tem, if po-tem, it comes after.
"ko" intros a mero-frasi-no-tem, single number. if plural, "plu" is also used.
"ge" is attached before the main word in a mero-frasi-tem. this indicates 
that the mero-frasi-po-tem (object) is before the mero-frasi-tem instead of 
after.
"the woman opened the door"   - "u gina pa aperi u porta"
"the door was opened by the woman"  - u porta pa ge-aperi per u gina"
also?
u porta pa subito ge aperi......
u porta ge subito aperi .....
ge porta pa aperi .....
???
I am trying to avoid using words such as:- present indicative, past historic, 
subjunctive, subject, indirect object, prepositional phrase, conditional 
clause, noun, verb adjective and a whole host of really juicy odds and ends 
that I've forgotten thankfully. I think that you'll find most of the above 
already in use in glosa so it's not too revolutionary.
sid 

--part1_135.509ffc5.292eb992_boundary
Content-Type: text/html; charset="US-ASCII"
Content-Transfer-Encoding: 7bit

<HTML><FONT FACE=arial,helvetica><FONT  SIZE=2>intro-vera should be intro-verba
<BR>basically, a g mero-frasi/part sentence would have:
<BR>intro-verba, word helping the next word, word helping the next word, head word, preposition phrases helping the head word. 
<BR>the next intro-verba or pre-loka-verba would point to the head word before it -
<BR>"the thin and very tall man with long and dark brown hair is ...... "
<BR>"the" introduces, "very" helps "tall", "thin" and "tall" help "man", "with" shows that "man" is the head word, "dark" helps "brown", "long" and "brown" help "hair", "is" begins the next part sentence.
<BR>"u tenu e fo alti andro ko longi e skoto bruno kapila es ...... &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"
<BR>plu de intro-verba:-
<BR>"pa" introduces a part sentence with time/mero-frasi-tem, also states relation between the time and the time of another part sentence 
<BR>"fu" does as "pa" does
<BR>"du" does as "pa" does but is less essential - as "es" is used almost always as mero-frasi-tem, it can be left out as the speaker wishes
<BR>"u" intros a mero-frasi-pre/po-tem, also carries single number
<BR>if pre-tem, it comes before the mero-frasi-tem, if po-tem, it comes after.
<BR>"ko" intros a mero-frasi-no-tem, single number. if plural, "plu" is also used.
<BR>"ge" is attached before the main word in a mero-frasi-tem. this indicates that the mero-frasi-po-tem (object) is before the mero-frasi-tem instead of after.
<BR>"the woman opened the door" &nbsp;&nbsp;- "u gina pa aperi u porta"
<BR>"the door was opened by the woman" &nbsp;- u porta pa ge-aperi per u gina"
<BR>also?
<BR>u porta pa subito ge aperi......
<BR>u porta ge subito aperi .....
<BR>ge porta pa aperi .....
<BR>???
<BR>I am trying to avoid using words such as:- present indicative, past historic, subjunctive, subject, indirect object, prepositional phrase, conditional clause, noun, verb adjective and a whole host of really juicy odds and ends that I've forgotten thankfully. I think that you'll find most of the above already in use in glosa so it's not too revolutionary.
<BR>sid </FONT></HTML>

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: 18 steps copyright?
From: Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Date: Sat, 24 Nov 2001 04:20:57 +1100

At 08:21 20/11/2001 -0800, Kevin Smith wrote:
>I wrote to ask Marcel if I could get permission to update
>the "18 steps" document to conform to my unofficial core
>list (www.qualitycode.com/glosa).
>
>His response politely suggested that perhaps I should not
>tamper with this classic work of art (which other folks are
>also working on at the moment), and that my time might be
>better spent updating some of the other material.
>[Hopefully that's a reasonable summary. Marcel?]
>
*  Yes, Kevin,
   As I found (~1982-1992) things were a bit fluid during the 'evolution'
of Glosa.  I was very relieved when the _GLOSA 6000_ (1992) was published.
   Then, I still had to check from front to back of this bi-directional
dictionary to make sure I had the right word .. but I accepted this as a
part of the teething troubles, and assumed that a cleaned up version of
_Glosa 6000(Revised)_ would be produced with the aid of computer sorting
and the making of a standardised lexicon.
   It didn't quite happen this way, and Paul Bartlett was the first to
proclaim loudly that there were at least two or three varying "Glosa 1000"
word-lists.
   What had been a problem of the excesses of creativity, had become a
serious 'marketing' dilemma.
   Then, I attempted to form a standard Glosa lexicon by reversing the
GLOSA-ENGLISH list from _GLOSA 6000_, and this only showed that there were
more synonyms than we had bargained for.  Ron and Wendy, in their
enthusiasm threw in more Greek and Latin roots than were warranted.

   That was when I presented the problem to the Glosalist seeking
assistance; and, although we had long discussions about the optimal length
of the desired lexicon revision, each tended to follow his own inclination,
and ultimately a range of Glosa electronic dictionaries was produced.

   I felt a little exhausted by this activity, and thought, as Ron had,
that usage would tend to knock the rough edges off the language, as we all
started communicating in Glosa and writing stories and articles using Glosa.

   BTW, Wendy Ashby asked me to submit items for the upcoming PLU GLOSA
NOTA, so I would suggest that everyone supports her in this, and sends in a
short fictional work or an article for publication.  My guess is that Wendy
will edit items to be in line with Glosa 6000 spelling and standard PGN usage.
 - - - - - - - - - 
   But this is all a long way from "18 Steps to Fluency in Glosa."

   Yes you were right to open the discussion to all.  Perhaps "18 Steps ...
" is a "classic work of art", but it can readily be bettered as a first
reader.  So, I welcome your efforts, Kevin, and suggest that you have a
look at other introductory language readers, to give you an idea of the
different possible approaches.  When "18 Steps ... " was written, it was
aimed at the African learner of Glosa, so it was published in inexpensive
paper format.  Also, the economy cut both ways: Ron and Wendy could afford
neither the production of a thick printed book, nor the postage to their
enthusiastic learners in Africa of a wieghty tome. 
   Now, taking the world view, I imagine that Internet format is the
least-cost mode, and that printed versions can be run from electronic
master copies.
   The Web version of something like "18 Steps ... " could include HTML
coded hypertext links, some of which could call up sound.  I still have not
got organised into the technology of putting sound on the Web, but some on
this list might have the technology to do it.  
   The world has changed a lot since the production of "18 Steps ... ," and
while it can still be used to good effect, future learning materials can
range from possibly a little better .. to exciting audiovisuals, probably
in electronic format.

>here it is:
>
>---
>
>In my mind, one of the very large obstacles to adoption of
>Glosa is that there are now FIVE distinct dialects out
>there:
>1. 18 steps/PGN
>2. Glosa Basic Reference
>3. GID core/basic
>4. Syd Pidd's reforms (trailing vowels)
>5. My own word list
>
>Someone who uses words from any of the five will have some
>difficulty communicating with any of the others. I hope
>that material created based on my list would be the most
>widely understandable because generally my list is a subset
>of the others.
>
*   Where would you place _GLOSA 6000_ in this schema?  Do you have a copy
of this 1992 publication?  If not, I will organise to get one to you: all I
need is your land address.
    I must visit your website to see how your word list looks.

>My big concern is that "18 Steps" is being listed as the
>MOST official source, and it's also the only lengthy
>tutorial available. The web page says it is "the best way
>to learn Glosa." It is also the most widely translated
>material. This will lead new people to learn from it
>first...at which point they will find many, many words
>which are not in any of the other references.
>
*   I did not realise this.  However, if the words they learn are valid
Glosa words, no harm is done.  I ought to peruse my copy, to see if these
words are instances of Old Glosa.

>If "18 Steps" is really an unchangable work of art, it
>should be described as that. People should NOT use it as a
>first step towards learning Glosa. They should only look
>back at it after they have learned "modern" Glosa to gain
>perspective.
>
*   Basically, there should be 'no turning back.'  The only reason for
studying the formative publications is for academic research of Glosa, and
the world is not doing that, yet.  So, "18 Steps ... " can stay in the
archives while a more up to date 'First Reader' - Glosa-wise and
educationally -  is prepared.

>If we actually want people to learn from "18 Steps", it
>must be updated, at least to GID Basic (Core would be
>better), if not to my own list. It is disturbing to realize
>that Russians will be learning what some of us consider to
>be an obsolete dialect of Glosa.
>
*   This is all painfully true.
    I mean we could have a Glosalist competition for the most suitable
update of the "18 Steps ... " as it stands.  This would serve to give us
all a look at the way the different members of the group interpret it into
current Glosa .. leading quickly, I would hope, to a concensus version,
that all agreed was the best line through the 'versions' of Glosa you
describe - with the whole exercise acting as a standardising influence, as
well.

>My other option is to create my own tutorial, from scratch,
>using my word list. Obviously this would be much more
>difficult.
>
*   Probably better, but involving more variables, and a longer production
time.  I would go with the concensus upgrade of the historical "18 Steps
... " as a first task; this could be completed before Christmas.

>So I close with these questions, to which I would really
>like to hear your own personal answers:
>
>What are your goals for the Glosa langauge and community? 
>
*  1. Development of community of fluent users of Glosa - possibly written
Glosa, at first, but subsequently exchanging recordings of spoken Glosa.
   2. Availability of a group of Glosa speakers, so that IF, or WHEN, world
leaders call for selection of an International Auxiliary Language, Glosa
will be represented by a team.
   3. Fun.  Global communication has to be fun or its not worth doing.
   4. A meeting of progressive minds.

>What are the one or two projects someone could do right now
>that would have the most impact towards those goals?
>
*  A. Preparation of one definitive Beginner's Lexicon - of possibly 2500
words, having synonyms for only a few common concepts.
   B. Preparation of a standard paper instruction book, but with standard
rules, reading passages, and short lists of questions for each chapter.
   C. Production of interesting Audio-visual sequences of Glosa
instruction, aimed at circulation through websites and on CD.
   D. Glosa comics.  This would be fun for the more visually oriented Glosa
learners.
   E. An active postal exchange of cassettes with spoken Glosa.

>What do you see as the biggest weaknesses of the language
>(and/or its community) right now? 
>
*  Language: Need for agreement on a standardised lexicon.
             As a concept-based language with syntax based grammar, Glosa
does not have the comfortable feel of a native language with all of the
usual inflections: speakers of Glosa have to think before they speak.
             The lack of a complete lexicon - all the vegetables,fruits,
animals, trees, etc. 
             Although well-written Glosa is a pleasure to read, because of
lack of morphological grammar, it is very extremely easy for the
over-confident learner to write very bad Glosa: the syntactic signposts are
in the head, not provided by visible and audible inflection.   

  Community: Appears to be stuck at the 'vocabulary formation' stage.
             Few fluent writers and speakers.
             African learners, especially in Uganda, are being starved of
contact with other Glosa-pe.
             + +  Development of Glosa segments to websites is a very
encouraging sign among the more affluent learners.

Saluta,

Robin Gaskell
  P.S. I feel that with a more positive global intellectual climate there
would be exponential growth of Glosa usage.  Also, I feel that in the same
way that the world's knowlege should be freely available to all, such
global information could be available in one language medium, and Glosa is
suitable for this task.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] 18 steps copyright?
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Fri, 23 Nov 2001 23:50:11 -0800

Robin wrote:

> but I accepted this as a
> part of the teething troubles, and assumed that a cleaned up version of
> _Glosa 6000(Revised)_ would be produced with the aid of computer sorting
> and the making of a standardised lexicon.

>              As a concept-based language with syntax based grammar, Glosa
> does not have the comfortable feel of a native language with all of the
> usual inflections: speakers of Glosa have to think before they speak.
>              The lack of a complete lexicon - all the vegetables,fruits,
> animals, trees, etc.

What's needed is an algorithm to select vocabulary. It could be re-run
periodically for updates. When I challenged the Interlingua "experts",
they were unable to describe any effective, repeatable, objective method.
But it's possible to derive the Glosa vocab according to clear principles
by a published computer program using public data sources.

Recently I've been experimenting with a "language sound change" program,
running English through it without regard to semantics, similar to how
language evolves. It's basically another "English spelling reform" but
with a few twists. Imitating what seems Ron Clark style, it simplifies
the alphabet and shoehorns all English words into easily pronounceable
CVCV-ish bisyllables. (C.S. people call it a hash table.)

The "euro-international" names of the days of the week are based on
astrology, sun-day moon-day mars-day mercury- jupiter- venus- saturn-.
But with usage they became smoothed out a bit. Here are some day names
cranked out by my program, just for fun, sorted by frequency:

26342   todu    today
19507   jeste   yesterday
9364    sundu   sunday
8310    satu    saturday
7783    joli    holiday
5540    fridu   friday
5286    mondu   monday
4406    vedne   wednesday
3722    fursu   thursday
3568    tesdu   tuesday
3247    birfu   birthday
1018    duli    daylight
555     midu    midday
536     duti    daytime
184     vekdu   weekday
49      dudra   daydream

This is just from the BNC corpus, English-only; one could include other
lists for a more international flavor. The words automatically "compete"
for their positions, no subjective judgement or hand-tuning is ever needed.
The top-3000 words would be the "core" list by definition. It looks like
8000 or so root-concept-words will cover the entire set (approx 100,000
distinct English words). This style of reform, basing decisions on an
objective basis, escapes the limitations of the "artlang" approach.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Glosa History and G6000 (was: 18 steps copyright?)
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 24 Nov 2001 07:26:56 -0800 (PST)

--- Robin Gaskell <rgaskell{at}zeta{dot}org{dot}au> wrote:
>    As I found (~1982-1992) things were a bit fluid 
> during the 'evolution' of Glosa.  I was very 
> relieved when the _GLOSA 6000_ (1992) was published.

Thanks for the history. Context aids understanding.

>    I felt a little exhausted by this activity, and
> thought, as Ron had, that usage would tend to knock 
> the rough edges off the language, as we all started 
> communicating in Glosa and writing stories and
> articles using Glosa.

I think one of the reasons this has not happened is because
there are too many words in G6000 and the full GID. As new
people learn a language, if they are given a wide choice of
words, each will naturally choose a variety of words.

I guess that's mostly because many people probably learn
Glosa by themselves, from word lists. There isn't enough
existing Glosa material, or enough ongoing discussions IN
Glosa for people to learn that way.

6000 words is far too many for everyday usage. I'm not sure
such a work should even exist, but if it does, people
should be discouraged from using it.

As you move beyond 1000-2000 words, almost all the
additions should be specific names of plants, animals, and
scientific terms, and/or compounds that build on existing
roots. We don't need twelve roots that mean "happy".

Kevin

Robin Gaskell wrote

> of Glosa.  I was very relieved when the _GLOSA 6000_ (1992) was published.

"GLOSA 6000" was published in April 1992.

The latest (3rd revised edition) of
"18 STEPS to FLUENCY in EURO-GLOSA"
was published in September 1992. This is the
version that Marcel has put extracts of on his site.

The book was first published in 1985, and also
appeared in a revised 2nd edition in 1989.

I appreciate this offer, but I don't think I have any use
for a 6000 word list. Honestly, I believe that in an IAL,
less is more. Easier to learn. Easier to remember. Easier
to look things up. Easier to be more clear (although less
precise) in your communications.

> I must visit your website to see how your word list

far. http://www.qualitycode.com/glosa

From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 24 Nov 2001 18:52:32 -0800 (PST)

>>What are your goals for the Glosa langauge and 
>>community?

>*  1. Development of community of fluent users of 
>Glosa - possibly written Glosa, at first, but
>subsequently exchanging recordings of spoken Glosa.

Me too. I hadn't considered the spoken part, but I suppose
I could enjoy internet voice chats in Glosa.

As I've said before, this is by far my biggest Glosa goal.
I want Glosa to be a viable medium for exchanging
information across languages, and to get there it needs to
build a larger base of speakers.

>   2. Availability of a group of Glosa speakers, so 
>that IF, or WHEN, world leaders call for selection of 
>an International Auxiliary Language, Glosa will be 
>represented by a team.

Optimistic and noble. I like it.

>   3. Fun.  Global communication has to be fun or its 
>not worth doing.

Absolutely.

>   4. A meeting of progressive minds.

Yes. Mental stimulation. Teamwork. Great.

Kevin

To: glosalist <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: [glosalist] Glosa projects (was: 18 steps copyright?)
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 24 Nov 2001 18:58:55 -0800 (PST)

>>What are the one or two projects someone could do 
>>right now that would have the most impact towards 
>>those goals?

>*  A. Preparation of one definitive Beginner's 
>Lexicon - of possibly 2500 words, having synonyms for 
>only a few common concepts.

I have made my attempt (~1300 roots, ~1600 entries). 

>   B. Preparation of a standard paper instruction 
>book, but with standard rules, reading passages, and 
>short lists of questions for each chapter.

Interesting. I have trouble imagining a paper book getting
much distribution. Do you have a particular target group or
geography in mind?

>   C. Production of interesting Audio-visual sequences 
>of Glosa instruction, aimed at circulation through 
>websites and on CD.
>   D. Glosa comics.  This would be fun for the more 
>visually oriented Glosa learners.
>   E. An active postal exchange of cassettes with 
>spoken Glosa.

Again, I hadn't thought of moving beyond the written word.
These would certainly give Glosa an unusual edge.

Other projects I am considering:
1. Creating a true Glosa Dictionary. Each Glosa word would
have a definition written in Glosa.

2. Adding additional translations (besides English) to my
word list. I think I could pretty easily tweak my software
to allow any number of languages to coexist in the same
file (Spanish, Russian, etc).

How valuable to you all think either of these would be?

Kevin

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Weaknesses (was: 18 steps copyright?)
From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>
Date: Sat, 24 Nov 2001 19:14:48 -0800 (PST)

>>What do you see as the biggest weaknesses of the 
>>language (and/or its community) right now?

>*  Language: Need for agreement on a standardised 
>lexicon.

Agreed. But how can we reach "standardization"? Would that
require Wendy's participation and approval? Or has the
language community grown to the point where the original
author(s) become just one voice among many?

Participants in this list do not seem to be active or
involved enough to actually "vote" on issues that come up.
When issues do arise, I don't think we reach consensus very
often.

>           The lack of a complete lexicon - all the
>vegetables,fruits, animals, trees, etc.

That would be interesting. I have thought about having
multiple "add-ons" to the core word list. If you are
interested in technical issues, add the technical list.
Some people might add the anatomy list, or the foods list,
or whatever. That way, the core list can remain simple.

>          Although well-written Glosa is a pleasure to 
>read, because of lack of morphological grammar, it is 
>very extremely easy for the over-confident learner to 
>write very bad Glosa: the syntactic signposts are
>in the head, not provided by visible and audible 
>inflection.     

Well put. Some of my early Glosa writing is unclear because
I kept too many signposts in my head, instead of explcitly
writing them out. I'm very slowly going back and updating
that text (and at the same time updating the vocabulary to
match my streamlined word list).

>Community: Appears to be stuck at the 'vocabulary 
>formation' stage.
>             Few fluent writers and speakers.

Agreed. There seem to be about five or ten of us here. And
in the long time I've been monitoring the list, I think
that number has only gone up by one or two.

>             African learners, especially in Uganda, 
>are being starved of contact with other Glosa-pe.

Very interesting. I wasn't even aware of them. I assume
they have no (or very limited) internet access. My own life
is so tightly bound to the computer, I'm not sure I could
help there. Perhaps we could set up some kind of system to
automatically route any in-Glosa postings to a monthly
printed newsletter?

---
I would add a couple more community weaknesses:
1. Aside from the recent and very welcome influx of Russian
speakers, we remain very English-centric. I would love to
have a Glosa site similar to the Romanova site, which
offers information in MANY languages.

2. The members of this list seem more interested in
learning about (or debating :-) Glosa, rather than actually
using it.

Kevin

To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Glosa dialects (was: 18 steps copyright?)
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk>
Date: Sun, 25 Nov 2001 11:07:01 -0000

Kevin Smith  wrote:
> > >In my mind, one of the very large obstacles to 
> > >adoption of Glosa is that there are now FIVE 
> > >distinct dialects out there:
> > >1. 18 steps/PGN
> > >2. Glosa Basic Reference
> > >3. GID core/basic
> > >4. Syd Pidd's reforms (trailing vowels)
> > >5. My own word list
> 
> > *Where would you place _GLOSA 6000_ in this schema? 
> 
> Oops. I forgot about those. I suspect G1000 would be pretty
> close to the first (18 steps/PGN), although I doubt it fits
> exactly. I would consider G6000 to be yet another, as it
> must have so many words as to be completely overwhelming.

I believe that all the GLOSA  6000 words are included in GID.

>                                         I don't think I have any use
> for a 6000 word list. Honestly, I believe that in an IAL,
> less is more. Easier to learn. Easier to remember. Easier
> to look things up. Easier to be more clear (although less
> precise) in your communications.

I agree. One of the major design aims of the Interglossa/Glosa
Project was to produce a language with "word economy".

See what Hogben had to say on the subject.
http://www.zeta.org.au/~rgaskell/paraleli.htm


The Glosa authors also very frequently stress that Glosa has
two vocabularies; Mega Glosa and Central Glosa.

Plu Glosa Nota 37 is a typical example

"We agree that to sell Glosa to bodies such as EEC, UN, 
UNESCO etc. MEGA-GLOSA, a large vocabulary is 
essential. Scientists, literary people and politicians require 
it. 

"We however insist that GLOSA 1000 must remain the 
Centre, the Heart of Glosa. Glosa is a truly International 
Auxiliary Language. It will appeal to the motivated Third 
World artisan only if it is simple to learn and accurate, and 
able to deal with all kinds of information exchange. Glosa 
will not be allowed to fall into the trap of becoming only 
a holiday language for wealthy people of the industrialized 
nations. The world will never be healthily united until 
accurate information is readily available to everyone 
everywhere, and everybody in the world has enough to eat, 
good shelter and a good all-round education - an education 
for living not merely for earning a living. The world has now 
the means (but not yet the will) to bring all this about quickly 
and thoroughly. 

"Europeans are used to large vocabularies, which can contribute 
to mis-understandings not only between nations but also 
between classes. We hope that although perhaps enjoying the 
variety and freedom of Mega Glosa people will at the same 
time work their way up to Central Glosa 1000."

To: <glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au>
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Re: Names of weekdays in Glosa
From: "Nick Hempshall" <nick{at}hempshall{dot}fsbusiness{dot}co{dot}uk>
Date: Sun, 25 Nov 2001 11:37:50 -0000

Sydpidd{at}aol{dot}com wrote
k for Christians di k1 =mondays as the creator rested on 7th
day=sabbath=sunday
j  for Jewish   di j1 = sunday
i for Islam    di i1=??
also years  k2001= now etc
?????

Whilst Christians, Jews and Muslims have their 'special' day of the week on
Sunday, Saturday and Friday respectively. They are all in agreement that
Sunday is the first day of the week.

Interestingly, Hebrew also uses numbers for the names of the days of the
week.

Slavs think of Friday as the fifth day, for example Russians call it
"pyatneetza". And also some calendar printers put Monday as the
first day of the week, but this is far from being universal practice.

Year numbering is more controversial. But "Common Era" dates (C.E.),
which are the same as A.D. dates, are the most international, and, of
course, are not actually related to the birth year of Jesus of Nazareth.

Thanks.

> However I'd like to merge it into some of my
> lexical collection but I can't extract it.

> possibly better if to me personally as it will be

I just added it to my website. However, it's currently in
the raw form which gets converted to HTML by a Ruby
computer program (also posted on my site). If you can
describe a simple text format (or XML) that you would like
me to convert it to, I can write a program to make it
available in that format as well.

> I had in mind some time ago, a g to e of a fairly small
> set of words like g1000+. These could be translated into
other 
> languages, 

This sounds exactly like what I have started. My plan is
that each Glosa entry in my list can have separate attached
information for each language. The Glosa field would
contain a definition written in Glosa. It would have
English, Spanish, Russian, and any number of additional
fields for additional languages.

My computer program could generate HTML which would contain
any single language, any two languages, or all languages.

There may be a way to have a program automatically do the
translation from the Glosa list, and then I could write a
program to pull the results back into the list
automatically. Then humans could correct any
mis-translations.

Kevin

Ah. Yes, I was right: it is overwhelming. So I would add
another dialect to my list:

6. G6000/Full GID

Hopefully G1000 is fairly similar to both 18 steps/PGN
and/or GID core/basic. I'm not sure if it's really close to
either, or if it would end up being dialect #7.

I'm using the word dialect as a layperson--I don't know if
it's technically correct from a linguistic viewpoint. Also,
it is really a gray area--I don't know if I would consider
something to be distinct only if it had a 1% variation in
words, or 20%. 

I just know that someone who uses 18 steps vocabulary will
have some trouble communicating with someone who uses GID
core (to pick just one example).

Kevin

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Glosa dialects (was: 18 steps copyright?)
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Sun, 25 Nov 2001 12:10:25 -0800

By the way, I object to calling each wordlist a "dialect".
It's especially unhelpful for an auxlang. Maybe if would be
fair to call Hogben's Interglossa one of the two dialects
of Glosa. Whoever takes over control of Glosa after Wendy
will probably generate the 3rd ...

> I had in mind some time ago, a g to e of a fairly small set of words like
> g1000+. These could be translated into other languages, I'm having some
> trouble with the translation programs, and the other g words would be g1000+
> to glosa explanations. I was working on a sort of thesaurus but Roget's seem
> rather high falluting for the job

Ron Clark did derive "Glosa 1000+" from Roget's Thesaurus.
There are now far better ways to select vocabulary, such as
EuroWordNet or other ideas I've discussed here previously.
The only wrong way to do it is to hand-pick as in an artlang.
For the survival of Glosa, and more so for any possible future
growth, it must become an ongoing committee-standardized and
regularly updated product (even if free, like Linux).

>It's especially unhelpful for an auxlang. Maybe if would be
>fair to call Hogben's Interglossa one of the two dialects
>of Glosa. Whoever takes over control of Glosa after Wendy
>will probably generate the 3rd ...
>   .....
>
>Ron Clark did derive "Glosa 1000+" from Roget's Thesaurus.
>There are now far better ways to select vocabulary, such as
>EuroWordNet or other ideas I've discussed here previously.
>The only wrong way to do it is to hand-pick as in an artlang.
>For the survival of Glosa, and more so for any possible future
>growth, it must become an ongoing committee-standardized and
>regularly updated product (even if free, like Linux).
>
*   I could not agree more.

    At present, the subscribers to Glosalist + Wendy Ashby comprise such a
"standardising committee."

    Were the members of Glosalist .. using a suitable standard list, such
as that from _EuroWordNet_ .. to be able to agree upon an approx. 1000 word
list for Glosa, that would be a start.

    This should not be an impossability, but it would suggest the need for
some painful thinking on the part of those wishing to "improve" upon the
various, existing basic Glosa word-lists.

    Were we to agree on using one standardised list, such as that suggested
by Charles, _EuroWordNet_, then we would certainly add credibility to the
exercise; this sort of thing takes Glosa from hobbyist level to committee
level, and would give the project more authority - as seen through the eyes
of UN and EC committees that decide on things like Universal Standards.

    Wendy Ashby would have to be informed of our actions before we started
on such a venture, because she would need to be part of the process: were
she on the Net, I would CC: this message to her; as things are, I will have
to print out and post hardcopy of our deliberations to her.

    Please Charles, is a suitable version of the EuroWordList available for
forwarding to the Glosalist as an attachment?  Do other members of this
list have an alternative standard that they feel is superior to that
proposed by Charles?  If the selection of a standard is the first step,
then such a decision should be possible within one month; alternatively, if
there is no objection, the basic EuroWordList can be agreed to by majority
vote, or concensus, within two to four weeks.

    While Wendy is not getting too old to wait for further delays, I, at
65, might be.  If we, as a group, are to get serious, and to phase up to
the professional level, then there is no room for the luxury of endless
discussion: I would like to bring the matter .. of the embarrassing range
of "Glosa 1000/Basic Glosa" lists .. to a head, and suggest that we work -
to proposed 'deadlines' - to arrive at one 'basic' list.

    IF we can do this, then we can press forward to derive standardised
lists at the "Glosa 2500" and "Glosa 6000" marks.  Of course the obvious
approach is to create one comprehensive lexicon, of say 6000-odd words, and
snip backwards, selecting "Basic" and "Learner" vocabularies from this.
However, such an approach might assume a singularity of purpose beyond that
of the Glosalist subscribership: I believe that the formation of the
smallest standardised list would 'test' our integrity as a group, and, as
such, might determine whether, or not, Glosa has a future.

    The technical language extensions to Glosa, suggested by Kevin Smith,
are vitally important to the growth of Glosa as a potential Global
Auxilliary; but, such extension need to be built onto a firm foundation;
it is the lack of a standardised lexicon that prevents many Glosa
enthusiasts from progressing into normal spoken use of the language.  Once
the foundation is reasonably well set (of course with rules for alterations
to the language with cultural change) there will be a redirection of energy
into the creation of a "corpus" (collection of original works written in
Glosa), and the language will start growing again.

Saluta,

Robin Gaskell
  P.S. Hardcopy mailed to Wendy Ashby.   R.G.

I want to be sure everyone realizes that I have two very
different word lists up on my site. First, I believe Robin
is referring to my "Techno" word list. I see this as an
adjunct, and not critical for Glosa's success.

The other (and more important) work is my set of three
"unofficial core" lists (Glosa-English-alphabetical,
Glosa-English-categorized, and English-Glosa-alphabetical).
This is the list that I would like to see replace (or
strongly influence) GID Core and/or G1000.

> *  I am a fossil, who prefers to use a paper reference

rough edges by usage. So a thorough re-modeling is needed.

that I know of (at least) one that does work well for me.

A more careful reading would not have lead to that assumption.
Glosa is better than Interglossa, but still needs more work.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] other IALs
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Mon, 26 Nov 2001 19:13:05 -0800

I don't want to discuss one IAL versus another, because
such discussions always become unpleasant disputations.
Actually, I like all of them, and so-called artlangs too.
My own experience to try many of them and eventually
settled on one as "best": Occidental, designed for those
who already know at least one other European language and
have at least studied some others. But if people want to
discuss other IALs, I urge that it be done off-list.

Glosa has a different idea, to use analytical grammar and
non-Latin roots, for a global audience that is non-European.
It more closely approximates a pidgin/creole like the
Lingua Franca or Chinook Jargon or even Indonesian, and
theoretically should be easiest to learn. I've seen a few
other projects somewhat like Glosa (Lingua Franca Nova, Tunu)
and believe that Glosa needs to be improved in many ways.

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] other IALs
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Mon, 26 Nov 2001 22:15:56 -0800

> Should I search the archives for the things you feel should
> be improved in Glosa? Or can you restate them briefly?
> (Aside from vocabulary

My opinions have evolved a lot, and continue to do so ...

Generally, Glosa principles need to be clearly defined.
Then it can be extended and updated by people other than
the author-artist. As it is, there are bits of everything
but no real system -- and lots of contradictions. In an
"engineered" language this is unacceptable, though as a
prototype it's an interesting experiment.

Should the "scientific vocabulary" and Latin prefixed verbs
and mangled Greek roots all be thrown together into the pot?

Should there be any affixes, and if so should they be
prefixed or suffixed? Why select derivational suffixes
like -DO when the full word is only 2 syllables and fits
the "no morphology except compounds" scheme? Why have
GE- for something like a past/passive participle but
not have any present/active participle? Etc.

As for vocabulary, why not stipulate that Roget's headwords
and/or Euronet's top ontology and/or Kanji-6000 as the
official set of roots? And then systematically relex them
to a pure bisyllabic CVCV scheme that fits the phonology?

And then let's see some realistic translations, without the
parochial English idioms, to compare with the equivalent text
in other languages.

Draconian pronouncements, e.g "all Greek roots" or "pure relex"
would be more acceptable to me than the current mish-mash.
Maybe what I'd like best is basically a renewed Lingua Franca.
Others will have very different preferences, naturally,
and it would be interesting to hear them.

are my emails coming through any better than they were? I have had one 
indication that it might be so and my PC has had some checking over and 
ajustments from mr Jenning's engineers.

a little rambling:-
glosa started as words from latin and greek plus syntax from england. This 
made it easy to use by europeans especially me, g is the easiest language I 
have come across.  The people who are using it and discussing it are , on the 
whole, a pretty brainy lot and can cope with several languages and technical 
terms etc. they tend to forget that that g should be aimed at simpler folk. 
But, g should become a language of its own, let us forget the latin, greek 
and english, it has, on various web sites and glosalist computers, a pretty 
comprehensive and useable vocabulary and hidden away to some extent, its own 
syntax. We should be making them all clear for our simpler folk like a 75 
year old englishman with patches of confusion and memory loss to trouble him.
Looking back over the last 60 years or so, g and glosalist can claim credit 
for a miracle or two, a german and englishman on friendly terms and working 
together, russians and americans doing the same - things were not always like 
that. Over the last few years I have exchanged ideas through glist and pgn 
and in g, with people from china, uganda, greece,   sweden, germany russia 
usa  for instance. g is a success , small perhaps but a success
it can stand alone, it has fledged, ............

about voting, voting is already taking place - I am a self appointed 
returning officer - much g, I have put on my PC the number of times each word 
is used is noted and I shall make a dictionary based on it to go with the 
welter of files from G1000, G6000, core and so on
sid

From: Kevin Smith <kevinbsmith{at}yahoo{dot}com>

--- Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com wrote:
> Generally, Glosa principles need to be clearly 
> defined. Then it can be extended and updated by 
> people other than the author-artist. 
> ...snipped more suggestions for ways to improve Glosa

Mi nu ze ski tu tende, sed a mi, tu nota du freqe feno
dice; tu volu face u holo neo lingua. Plu proposi de tu du
feno (a mi) mega muta--homo Eo->Ido, alo posi ma mega de
u-la.
 
[I don't know if it's your intention, but I keep reading
your messages and hearing that you want to create a new
language. The improvements you suggest seem (to me) to be
huge reforms--on the order of Eo->Ido (or perhaps even more
dramatic.]

Mi fu posi:
1. Tena Glosa, e gradu tenta duce id a fu onto u ma-boni
lingua, ALO
2. Ki ab Glosa, e uti u hetero auxi-lingua, qi sio feno
ma-proxi a perfekti, ALO
3. Face u neo lingua.

[Personally, I will either:
1. stick with Glosa, and attempt to gradually steer it to
be a better language, OR 
2. abandon it in favor of some other IAL which seems closer
to my idea of perfection, OR 
3. create my own IAL.]

Alo mi fu posi tenta sucede poli de plu-la mode, iso-tem.

[Or perhaps I will attempt to do more than one of those at
the same time.]

> And then let's see some realistic translations, 
> without the parochial English idioms, to compare 
> with the equivalent text in other languages.

Je. Na du nece ma Glosa grafo-ra, e ma ge-grafo per ze-ski
English-pe.

[Yes. We need more Glosa material, and more written by
non-English speakers.]

Kevin

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Glosa dialects (was: 18 steps copyright?)
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Thu, 29 Nov 2001 12:20:02 -0800

> Were the members of Glosalist .. using a suitable standard list, such
> as that from _EuroWordNet_ .. to be able to agree upon an approx. 1000 word
> list for Glosa, that would be a start.

There is a mass of data and it's easy to get lost in it;
prepare to wade through a lot of academic bureaucratese ...
what we want is the "1024 Common Base Concepts".

http://www.hum.uva.nl/~ewn/data.htm
http://www.hum.uva.nl/~ewn/corebcs/topont.htm
http://www.hum.uva.nl/~ewn/corebcs/corebcs.html (164 top concepts)
http://www.hum.uva.nl/~ewn/corebcs/ewnTopOntology.htm
http://www.hum.uva.nl/~ewn/corebcs/AbstractInfo.htm

> This should not be an impossability, but it would suggest the need for
> some painful thinking on the part of those wishing to "improve" upon the
> various, existing basic Glosa word-lists.

At this point we should be adding words, not subtracting any.
But the personal efforts to identify "core" subsets are a good
exercise. In a full dictionary, the 1000 most frequently used
words should be highlighted. IMHO.

> Do other members of this
> list have an alternative standard that they feel is superior

There are several other good options, and they could all be
done independently and additively. The 6000 Kanji taught to
all Japanese students are a very good alternative. And the
example of Tunu shows how this might look:

a/an            SpE LDV SFL BNC  bija
ability/able    SpE LDV SFL BNC  nilo
about           SpE LDV SFL BNC  naju
above           SpE LDV SFL BNC  bamo / situ
abroad              LDV          mite
absence/absent      LDV          lalaju

The author has taken Rick Harrison's meta-list of English
vocabulary, which includes Special English, top-1000 of BNC,
Longman's Defining set, and Eaton's list ... at my suggestion,
BTW ... and provided the Tunu equivalents on the right. Tunu
is another analytic style IAL like Glosa.

I have several other English vocab lists from the time we last
discussed "what is core?" on this e-list. Let 1000 flowers bloom.

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: Re: [glosalist] Weaknesses (was: 18 steps copyright?)
From: Sydpidd{at}aol{dot}com
Date: Thu, 29 Nov 2001 15:20:08 EST

--part1_134.5685269.2937f278_boundary
Content-Type: text/plain; charset="US-ASCII"
Content-Transfer-Encoding: 7bit

this is from "recruitable" a publication by an organisation to help get 
people back to work. It is for us simpler folk and written for us in a simple 
direct english which would be very useful to translate into g. very nice 
english too.

"Recruitable helps people who want to work, to find a job"
l
exicon:-
R, auxi, pe, qi, volu, ergo, te, detekti, u, ergo-topo

let us try:-
pa auxi/helped     ok
pa pe/personed   ???
qi=intro-verba/intro-word,particle         no pa
pa volu/wished    ok
pa ergo/worked   here ok
te=intro-verba
pa detekti/found  ok
u=intro-verba
pa ergo-loka/workplaced     ???

auxi, volu, ergo, detekti are tem-mero-frasi/tem-verba-grupo/verbs
auxi/volu-ergo and detekti need intro words/particles
glosa= du auxi, du volu ergo, te detekti
R is earlier than auxi, qi is earlier than volu-ergo, and also earlier than 
detekti so precede those verb phrases
pe is later than auxi, ergo-loka is later than detekti so are after the verb 
phrases
pe and ergo-loka need intro-words
plu pe, u ergo-loka

so:-
"R  du auxi plu pe qi volu ergo te detekti plu ergo-loka"
the intro-words make it much easier and quicker to understand the sentence, 
no-qe?
sid

--part1_134.5685269.2937f278_boundary

To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] Glosa dialects (was: 18 steps copyright?)
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Thu, 29 Nov 2001 14:42:25 -0800

> what we want is the "1024 Common Base Concepts".

I just uploaded a reformatted list derived from the
EWN data files: http://catty.com/~catty/lang/bc.dat
and it looks like this using "set tabstop=20" in vi ...

Variant-1:          aim 2
Gloss:              the goal intended to be hit
Hyperonym:          end 5
TO3:                MentalObject
Literal:            04029556-n
SituationType:      3rdOrderEntity
Cause:              Stimulating
Phys/Ment:          Mental
Purpose:            Purpose

Variant-1:          aim 3
Gloss:              an anticipated outcome that guides your planned actions
Hyperonym:          end 5
TO3:                MentalObject
Literal:            04030116-n
SituationType:      3rdOrderEntity
Cause:              Stimulating
Phys/Ment:          Mental
Purpose:            Purpose

.. etc. Not user-friendly, but tractable and meaty.
The most important fields are just the top 4 (above "Literal")
but I included them all (except, empty fields were deleted).

========
To: glosalist{at}lists{dot}zeta{dot}org{dot}au
Subject: [glosalist] vocab lists
From: Charles{at}Catty{dot}Com
Date: Fri, 30 Nov 2001 10:50:30 -0800

I revisited the lists done in august 2000. They were 7 lists
of "essential" English vocabulary compiled by various people.
Any dictionary for Glosa would have to translate at least the
top few thousand items that appear in most of them, and I feel
that they should all be one-root words rather than compounds.

The sources were:
Paul Bartlett's list
Rick Harrison's metalist
Tunu vocab
General Service List
Top 10000 from the BNC
Kilgariff's list
Yang's learner list

I've now added the EuroWordNet set, but it wasn't very useful;
only 117 new entries, and just 650 or so in total. All 8 lists
were merged/sorted, and a count made of how many lists included
each word. I think that adding 100 more lists would not change
the results much.

193 words appear on all 8 lists, 832 appear on 7 (or more) lists,
1532 are on 6+ of the lists, 1990 on 5+, 2441 on 4+, 3310 on 3+,
5437 on 2+, 9629 in all. Those that appear only once include
some flaky stuff like abbreviations; the 3-or-mores look pretty
reliable. Anyway, it's uploaded for any who care, at:
http://catty.com/~catty/lang/engcore
